__label__twp The other guests were all conversing with one another.
__label__twp “The Razumóvskis...
__label__twp It was charming...
__label__twp You are very kind...
__label__twp Countess Apráksina...” was heard on all sides.
__label__twp The countess rose and went into the ballroom.
__label__twp “Márya Dmítrievna?” came her voice from there.
__label__twp “Herself,” came the answer in a rough voice, and Márya Dmítrievna entered the room.
__label__twp All the unmarried ladies and even the married ones except the very oldest rose.
__label__twp Márya Dmítrievna paused at the door.
__label__twp Tall and stout, holding high her fifty-year-old head with its gray curls, she stood surveying the guests, and leisurely arranged her wide sleeves as if rolling them up.
__label__twp Márya Dmítrievna always spoke in Russian.
__label__twp “Health and happiness to her whose name day we are keeping and to her children,” she said, in her loud, full-toned voice which drowned all others.
__label__twp “Well, you old sinner,” she went on, turning to the count who was kissing her hand, “you’re feeling dull in Moscow, I daresay? Nowhere to hunt with your dogs? But what is to be done, old man? Just see how these nestlings are growing up,” and she pointed to the girls.
__label__twp “You must look for husbands for them whether you like it or not....”  “Well,” said she, “how’s my Cossack?” (Márya Dmítrievna always called Natásha a Cossack) and she stroked the child’s arm as she came up fearless and gay to kiss her hand.
__label__twp “I know she’s a scamp of a girl, but I like her.”  She took a pair of pear-shaped ruby earrings from her huge reticule and, having given them to the rosy Natásha, who beamed with the pleasure of her saint’s-day fete, turned away at once and addressed herself to Pierre.
__label__twp “Eh, eh, friend! Come here a bit,” said she, assuming a soft high tone of voice.
__label__twp “Come here, my friend...” and she ominously tucked up her sleeves still higher.
__label__twp Pierre approached, looking at her in a childlike way through his spectacles.
__label__twp “Come nearer, come nearer, friend! I used to be the only one to tell your father the truth when he was in favor, and in your case it’s my evident duty.” She paused.
__label__twp All were silent, expectant of what was to follow, for this was clearly only a prelude.
__label__twp “A fine lad! My word! A fine lad!...
__label__twp His father lies on his deathbed and he amuses himself setting a policeman astride a bear! For shame, sir, for shame! It would be better if you went to the war.”  She turned away and gave her hand to the count, who could hardly keep from laughing.
__label__twp “Well, I suppose it is time we were at table?” said Márya Dmítrievna.
__label__twp The count went in first with Márya Dmítrievna, the countess followed on the arm of a colonel of hussars, a man of importance to them because Nicholas was to go with him to the regiment; then came Anna Mikháylovna with Shinshín.
__label__twp Berg gave his arm to Véra.
__label__twp The smiling Julie Karágina went in with Nicholas.
__label__twp After them other couples followed, filling the whole dining hall, and last of all the children, tutors, and governesses followed singly.
__label__twp The footmen began moving about, chairs scraped, the band struck up in the gallery, and the guests settled down in their places.
__label__twp Then the strains of the count’s household band were replaced by the clatter of knives and forks, the voices of visitors, and the soft steps of the footmen.
__label__twp At one end of the table sat the countess with Márya Dmítrievna on her right and Anna Mikháylovna on her left, the other lady visitors were farther down.
__label__twp At the other end sat the count, with the hussar colonel on his left and Shinshín and the other male visitors on his right.
__label__twp Midway down the long table on one side sat the grown-up young people: Véra beside Berg, and Pierre beside Borís; and on the other side, the children, tutors, and governesses.
__label__twp From behind the crystal decanters and fruit vases, the count kept glancing at his wife and her tall cap with its light-blue ribbons, and busily filled his neighbors’ glasses, not neglecting his own.
__label__twp The countess in turn, without omitting her duties as hostess, threw significant glances from behind the pineapples at her husband whose face and bald head seemed by their redness to contrast more than usual with his gray hair.
__label__twp At the ladies’ end an even chatter of voices was heard all the time, at the men’s end the voices sounded louder and louder, especially that of the colonel of hussars who, growing more and more flushed, ate and drank so much that the count held him up as a pattern to the other guests.
__label__twp Berg with tender smiles was saying to Véra that love is not an earthly but a heavenly feeling.
__label__twp Borís was telling his new friend Pierre who the guests were and exchanging glances with Natásha, who was sitting opposite.
__label__twp Pierre spoke little but examined the new faces, and ate a great deal.
__label__twp Of the two soups he chose turtle with savory patties and went on to the game without omitting a single dish or one of the wines.
__label__twp These latter the butler thrust mysteriously forward, wrapped in a napkin, from behind the next man’s shoulders and whispered: “Dry Madeira”...
__label__twp “Hungarian”...
__label__twp or “Rhine wine” as the case might be.
__label__twp Of the four crystal glasses engraved with the count’s monogram that stood before his plate, Pierre held out one at random and drank with enjoyment, gazing with ever-increasing amiability at the other guests.
__label__twp Natásha, who sat opposite, was looking at Borís as girls of thirteen look at the boy they are in love with and have just kissed for the first time.
__label__twp Sometimes that same look fell on Pierre, and that funny lively little girl’s look made him inclined to laugh without knowing why.
__label__twp Nicholas sat at some distance from Sónya, beside Julie Karágina, to whom he was again talking with the same involuntary smile.
__label__twp Sónya wore a company smile but was evidently tormented by jealousy; now she turned pale, now blushed and strained every nerve to overhear what Nicholas and Julie were saying to one another.
__label__twp The governess kept looking round uneasily as if preparing to resent any slight that might be put upon the children.
__label__twp The German tutor was trying to remember all the dishes, wines, and kinds of dessert, in order to send a full description of the dinner to his people in Germany; and he felt greatly offended when the butler with a bottle wrapped in a napkin passed him by.
__label__twp He frowned, trying to appear as if he did not want any of that wine, but was mortified because no one would understand that it was not to quench his thirst or from greediness that he wanted it, but simply from a conscientious desire for knowledge.
__label__twp CHAPTER XIX  At the men’s end of the table the talk grew more and more animated.
__label__twp The colonel told them that the declaration of war had already appeared in Petersburg and that a copy, which he had himself seen, had that day been forwarded by courier to the commander in chief.
__label__twp “And why the deuce are we going to fight Bonaparte?” remarked Shinshín.
__label__twp “He has stopped Austria’s cackle and I fear it will be our turn next.”  The colonel was a stout, tall, plethoric German, evidently devoted to the service and patriotically Russian.
__label__twp He resented Shinshín’s remark.
__label__twp “It is for the reasson, my goot sir,” said he, speaking with a German accent, “for the reasson zat ze Emperor knows zat.
__label__twp He declares in ze manifessto zat he cannot fiew wiz indifference ze danger vreatening Russia and zat ze safety and dignity of ze Empire as vell as ze sanctity of its alliances...” he spoke this last word with particular emphasis as if in it lay the gist of the matter.
__label__twp Then with the unerring official memory that characterized him he repeated from the opening words of the manifesto:  ...
__label__twp and the wish, which constitutes the Emperor’s sole and absolute aim—to establish peace in Europe on firm foundations—has now decided him to despatch part of the army abroad and to create a new condition for the attainment of that purpose.
__label__twp “Zat, my dear sir, is vy...” he concluded, drinking a tumbler of wine with dignity and looking to the count for approval.
__label__twp “Connaissez-vous le Proverbe:* ‘Jerome, Jerome, do not roam, but turn spindles at home!’?” said Shinshín, puckering his brows and smiling.
__label__twp “Cela nous convient à merveille.*(2) Suvórov now—he knew what he was about; yet they beat him à plate couture,*(3) and where are we to find Suvórovs now? Je vous demande un peu,” *(4) said he, continually changing from French to Russian.
__label__twp *Do you know the proverb?       *(2) That suits us down to the ground.
__label__twp *(3) Hollow.
__label__twp *(4) I just ask you that.
__label__twp “Ve must vight to the last tr-r-op of our plood!” said the colonel, thumping the table; “and ve must tie for our Emperor, and zen all vill pe vell.
__label__twp And ve must discuss it as little as po-o-ossible”...
__label__twp he dwelt particularly on the word possible...
__label__twp “as po-o-ossible,” he ended, again turning to the count.
__label__twp “Zat is how ve old hussars look at it, and zere’s an end of it! And how do you, a young man and a young hussar, how do you judge of it?” he added, addressing Nicholas, who when he heard that the war was being discussed had turned from his partner with eyes and ears intent on the colonel.
__label__twp “I am quite of your opinion,” replied Nicholas, flaming up, turning his plate round and moving his wineglasses about with as much decision and desperation as though he were at that moment facing some great danger.
__label__twp “I am convinced that we Russians must die or conquer,” he concluded, conscious—as were others—after the words were uttered that his remarks were too enthusiastic and emphatic for the occasion and were therefore awkward.
__label__twp “What you said just now was splendid!” said his partner Julie.
__label__twp Sónya trembled all over and blushed to her ears and behind them and down to her neck and shoulders while Nicholas was speaking.
__label__twp Pierre listened to the colonel’s speech and nodded approvingly.
__label__twp “That’s fine,” said he.
__label__twp “The young man’s a real hussar!” shouted the colonel, again thumping the table.
__label__twp “What are you making such a noise about over there?” Márya Dmítrievna’s deep voice suddenly inquired from the other end of the table.
__label__twp “What are you thumping the table for?” she demanded of the hussar, “and why are you exciting yourself? Do you think the French are here?”  “I am speaking ze truce,” replied the hussar with a smile.
__label__twp “It’s all about the war,” the count shouted down the table.
__label__twp “You know my son’s going, Márya Dmítrievna? My son is going.”  “I have four sons in the army but still I don’t fret.
__label__twp It is all in God’s hands.
__label__twp You may die in your bed or God may spare you in a battle,” replied Márya Dmítrievna’s deep voice, which easily carried the whole length of the table.
__label__twp “That’s true!”  Once more the conversations concentrated, the ladies’ at the one end and the men’s at the other.
__label__twp “You won’t ask,” Natásha’s little brother was saying; “I know you won’t ask!”  “I will,” replied Natásha.
__label__twp Her face suddenly flushed with reckless and joyous resolution.
__label__twp She half rose, by a glance inviting Pierre, who sat opposite, to listen to what was coming, and turning to her mother:  “Mamma!” rang out the clear contralto notes of her childish voice, audible the whole length of the table.
__label__twp “What is it?” asked the countess, startled; but seeing by her daughter’s face that it was only mischief, she shook a finger at her sternly with a threatening and forbidding movement of her head.
__label__twp The conversation was hushed.
__label__twp “Mamma! What sweets are we going to have?” and Natásha’s voice sounded still more firm and resolute.
__label__twp The countess tried to frown, but could not.
__label__twp Márya Dmítrievna shook her fat finger.
__label__twp “Cossack!” she said threateningly.
__label__twp Most of the guests, uncertain how to regard this sally, looked at the elders.
__label__twp “You had better take care!” said the countess.
__label__twp “Mamma! What sweets are we going to have?” Natásha again cried boldly, with saucy gaiety, confident that her prank would be taken in good part.
__label__twp Sónya and fat little Pétya doubled up with laughter.
__label__twp “You see! I have asked,” whispered Natásha to her little brother and to Pierre, glancing at him again.
__label__twp “Ice pudding, but you won’t get any,” said Márya Dmítrievna.
__label__twp Natásha saw there was nothing to be afraid of and so she braved even Márya Dmítrievna.
__label__twp “Márya Dmítrievna! What kind of ice pudding? I don’t like ice cream.”  “Carrot ices.”  “No! What kind, Márya Dmítrievna? What kind?” she almost screamed; “I want to know!”  Márya Dmítrievna and the countess burst out laughing, and all the guests joined in.
__label__twp Everyone laughed, not at Márya Dmítrievna’s answer but at the incredible boldness and smartness of this little girl who had dared to treat Márya Dmítrievna in this fashion.
__label__twp Natásha only desisted when she had been told that there would be pineapple ice.
__label__twp Before the ices, champagne was served round.
__label__twp The band again struck up, the count and countess kissed, and the guests, leaving their seats, went up to “congratulate” the countess, and reached across the table to clink glasses with the count, with the children, and with one another.
__label__twp Again the footmen rushed about, chairs scraped, and in the same order in which they had entered but with redder faces, the guests returned to the drawing room and to the count’s study.
__label__twp CHAPTER XX  The card tables were drawn out, sets made up for boston, and the count’s visitors settled themselves, some in the two drawing rooms, some in the sitting room, some in the library.
__label__twp The count, holding his cards fanwise, kept himself with difficulty from dropping into his usual after-dinner nap, and laughed at everything.
__label__twp The young people, at the countess’ instigation, gathered round the clavichord and harp.
__label__twp Julie by general request played first.
__label__twp After she had played a little air with variations on the harp, she joined the other young ladies in begging Natásha and Nicholas, who were noted for their musical talent, to sing something.
__label__twp Natásha, who was treated as though she were grown up, was evidently very proud of this but at the same time felt shy.
__label__twp “What shall we sing?” she said.
__label__twp “‘The Brook,’” suggested Nicholas.
__label__twp “Well, then, let’s be quick.
__label__twp Borís, come here,” said Natásha.
__label__twp “But where is Sónya?”  She looked round and seeing that her friend was not in the room ran to look for her.
__label__twp Running into Sónya’s room and not finding her there, Natásha ran to the nursery, but Sónya was not there either.
__label__twp Natásha concluded that she must be on the chest in the passage.
__label__twp The chest in the passage was the place of mourning for the younger female generation in the Rostóv household.
__label__twp And there in fact was Sónya lying face downward on Nurse’s dirty feather bed on the top of the chest, crumpling her gauzy pink dress under her, hiding her face with her slender fingers, and sobbing so convulsively that her bare little shoulders shook.
__label__twp Natásha’s face, which had been so radiantly happy all that saint’s day, suddenly changed: her eyes became fixed, and then a shiver passed down her broad neck and the corners of her mouth drooped.
__label__twp “Sónya! What is it? What is the matter?...
__label__twp Oo...
__label__twp Oo...
__label__twp Oo...!” And Natásha’s large mouth widened, making her look quite ugly, and she began to wail like a baby without knowing why, except that Sónya was crying.
__label__twp Sónya tried to lift her head to answer but could not, and hid her face still deeper in the bed.
__label__twp Natásha wept, sitting on the blue-striped feather bed and hugging her friend.
__label__twp With an effort Sónya sat up and began wiping her eyes and explaining.
__label__twp “Nicholas is going away in a week’s time, his...
__label__twp papers...
__label__twp have come...
__label__twp he told me himself...
__label__twp but still I should not cry,” and she showed a paper she held in her hand—with the verses Nicholas had written, “still, I should not cry, but you can’t...
__label__twp no one can understand...
__label__twp what a soul he has!”  And she began to cry again because he had such a noble soul.
__label__twp “It’s all very well for you...
__label__twp I am not envious...
__label__twp I love you and Borís also,” she went on, gaining a little strength; “he is nice...
__label__twp there are no difficulties in your way....
__label__twp But Nicholas is my cousin...
__label__twp one would have to...
__label__twp the Metropolitan himself...
__label__twp and even then it can’t be done.
__label__twp And besides, if she tells Mamma” (Sónya looked upon the countess as her mother and called her so) “that I am spoiling Nicholas’ career and am heartless and ungrateful, while truly...
__label__twp God is my witness,” and she made the sign of the cross, “I love her so much, and all of you, only Véra...
__label__twp And what for? What have I done to her? I am so grateful to you that I would willingly sacrifice everything, only I have nothing....”  Sónya could not continue, and again hid her face in her hands and in the feather bed.
__label__twp Natásha began consoling her, but her face showed that she understood all the gravity of her friend’s trouble.
__label__twp “Sónya,” she suddenly exclaimed, as if she had guessed the true reason of her friend’s sorrow, “I’m sure Véra has said something to you since dinner? Hasn’t she?”  “Yes, these verses Nicholas wrote himself and I copied some others, and she found them on my table and said she’d show them to Mamma, and that I was ungrateful, and that Mamma would never allow him to marry me, but that he’ll marry Julie.
__label__twp You see how he’s been with her all day...
__label__twp Natásha, what have I done to deserve it?...”  And again she began to sob, more bitterly than before.
__label__twp Natásha lifted her up, hugged her, and, smiling through her tears, began comforting her.
__label__twp “Sónya, don’t believe her, darling! Don’t believe her! Do you remember how we and Nicholas, all three of us, talked in the sitting room after supper? Why, we settled how everything was to be.
__label__twp I don’t quite remember how, but don’t you remember that it could all be arranged and how nice it all was? There’s Uncle Shinshín’s brother has married his first cousin.
__label__twp And we are only second cousins, you know.
__label__twp And Borís says it is quite possible.
__label__twp You know I have told him all about it.
__label__twp And he is so clever and so good!” said Natásha.
__label__twp “Don’t you cry, Sónya, dear love, darling Sónya!” and she kissed her and laughed.
__label__twp “Véra’s spiteful; never mind her! And all will come right and she won’t say anything to Mamma.
__label__twp Nicholas will tell her himself, and he doesn’t care at all for Julie.”  Natásha kissed her on the hair.
__label__twp Sónya sat up.
__label__twp The little kitten brightened, its eyes shone, and it seemed ready to lift its tail, jump down on its soft paws, and begin playing with the ball of worsted as a kitten should.
__label__twp “Do you think so?...
__label__twp Really? Truly?” she said, quickly smoothing her frock and hair.
__label__twp “Really, truly!” answered Natásha, pushing in a crisp lock that had strayed from under her friend’s plaits.
__label__twp Both laughed.
__label__twp “Well, let’s go and sing ‘The Brook.’”  “Come along!”  “Do you know, that fat Pierre who sat opposite me is so funny!” said Natásha, stopping suddenly.
__label__twp “I feel so happy!”  And she set off at a run along the passage.
__label__twp Sónya, shaking off some down which clung to her and tucking away the verses in the bosom of her dress close to her bony little chest, ran after Natásha down the passage into the sitting room with flushed face and light, joyous steps.
__label__twp At the visitors’ request the young people sang the quartette, “The Brook,” with which everyone was delighted.
__label__twp Then Nicholas sang a song he had just learned:     At nighttime in the moon’s fair glow      How sweet, as fancies wander free,    To feel that in this world there’s one      Who still is thinking but of thee!     That while her fingers touch the harp      Wafting sweet music o’er the lea,    It is for thee thus swells her heart,      Sighing its message out to thee...
__label__twp A day or two, then bliss unspoilt,      But oh! till then I cannot live!...
__label__twp He had not finished the last verse before the young people began to get ready to dance in the large hall, and the sound of the feet and the coughing of the musicians were heard from the gallery.
__label__twp Pierre was sitting in the drawing room where Shinshín had engaged him, as a man recently returned from abroad, in a political conversation in which several others joined but which bored Pierre.
__label__twp When the music began Natásha came in and walking straight up to Pierre said, laughing and blushing:  “Mamma told me to ask you to join the dancers.”  “I am afraid of mixing the figures,” Pierre replied; “but if you will be my teacher...” And lowering his big arm he offered it to the slender little girl.
__label__twp While the couples were arranging themselves and the musicians tuning up, Pierre sat down with his little partner.
__label__twp Natásha was perfectly happy; she was dancing with a grown-up man, who had been abroad.
__label__twp She was sitting in a conspicuous place and talking to him like a grown-up lady.
__label__twp She had a fan in her hand that one of the ladies had given her to hold.
__label__twp Assuming quite the pose of a society woman (heaven knows when and where she had learned it) she talked with her partner, fanning herself and smiling over the fan.
__label__twp “Dear, dear! Just look at her!” exclaimed the countess as she crossed the ballroom, pointing to Natásha.
__label__twp Natásha blushed and laughed.
__label__twp “Well, really, Mamma! Why should you? What is there to be surprised at?”   In the midst of the third écossaise there was a clatter of chairs being pushed back in the sitting room where the count and Márya Dmítrievna had been playing cards with the majority of the more distinguished and older visitors.
__label__twp They now, stretching themselves after sitting so long, and replacing their purses and pocketbooks, entered the ballroom.
__label__twp First came Márya Dmítrievna and the count, both with merry countenances.
__label__twp The count, with playful ceremony somewhat in ballet style, offered his bent arm to Márya Dmítrievna.
__label__twp He drew himself up, a smile of debonair gallantry lit up his face and as soon as the last figure of the écossaise was ended, he clapped his hands to the musicians and shouted up to their gallery, addressing the first violin:  “Semën! Do you know the Daniel Cooper?”  This was the count’s favorite dance, which he had danced in his youth.
__label__twp (Strictly speaking, Daniel Cooper was one figure of the anglaise.)  “Look at Papa!” shouted Natásha to the whole company, and quite forgetting that she was dancing with a grown-up partner she bent her curly head to her knees and made the whole room ring with her laughter.
__label__twp And indeed everybody in the room looked with a smile of pleasure at the jovial old gentleman, who standing beside his tall and stout partner, Márya Dmítrievna, curved his arms, beat time, straightened his shoulders, turned out his toes, tapped gently with his foot, and, by a smile that broadened his round face more and more, prepared the onlookers for what was to follow.
__label__twp As soon as the provocatively gay strains of Daniel Cooper (somewhat resembling those of a merry peasant dance) began to sound, all the doorways of the ballroom were suddenly filled by the domestic serfs—the men on one side and the women on the other—who with beaming faces had come to see their master making merry.
__label__twp “Just look at the master! A regular eagle he is!” loudly remarked the nurse, as she stood in one of the doorways.
__label__twp The count danced well and knew it.
__label__twp But his partner could not and did not want to dance well.
__label__twp Her enormous figure stood erect, her powerful arms hanging down (she had handed her reticule to the countess), and only her stern but handsome face really joined in the dance.
__label__twp What was expressed by the whole of the count’s plump figure, in Márya Dmítrievna found expression only in her more and more beaming face and quivering nose.
__label__twp But if the count, getting more and more into the swing of it, charmed the spectators by the unexpectedness of his adroit maneuvers and the agility with which he capered about on his light feet, Márya Dmítrievna produced no less impression by slight exertions—the least effort to move her shoulders or bend her arms when turning, or stamp her foot—which everyone appreciated in view of her size and habitual severity.
__label__twp The dance grew livelier and livelier.
__label__twp The other couples could not attract a moment’s attention to their own evolutions and did not even try to do so.
__label__twp All were watching the count and Márya Dmítrievna.
__label__twp Natásha kept pulling everyone by sleeve or dress, urging them to “look at Papa!” though as it was they never took their eyes off the couple.
__label__twp In the intervals of the dance the count, breathing deeply, waved and shouted to the musicians to play faster.
__label__twp Faster, faster, and faster; lightly, more lightly, and yet more lightly whirled the count, flying round Márya Dmítrievna, now on his toes, now on his heels; until, turning his partner round to her seat, he executed the final pas, raising his soft foot backwards, bowing his perspiring head, smiling and making a wide sweep with his arm, amid a thunder of applause and laughter led by Natásha.
__label__twp Both partners stood still, breathing heavily and wiping their faces with their cambric handkerchiefs.
__label__twp “That’s how we used to dance in our time, ma chère,” said the count.
__label__twp “That was a Daniel Cooper!” exclaimed Márya Dmítrievna, tucking up her sleeves and puffing heavily.
__label__twp CHAPTER XXI  While in the Rostóvs’ ballroom the sixth anglaise was being danced, to a tune in which the weary musicians blundered, and while tired footmen and cooks were getting the supper, Count Bezúkhov had a sixth stroke.
__label__twp The doctors pronounced recovery impossible.
__label__twp After a mute confession, communion was administered to the dying man, preparations made for the sacrament of unction, and in his house there was the bustle and thrill of suspense usual at such moments.
__label__twp Outside the house, beyond the gates, a group of undertakers, who hid whenever a carriage drove up, waited in expectation of an important order for an expensive funeral.
__label__twp The Military Governor of Moscow, who had been assiduous in sending aides-de-camp to inquire after the count’s health, came himself that evening to bid a last farewell to the celebrated grandee of Catherine’s court, Count Bezúkhov.
__label__twp The magnificent reception room was crowded.
__label__twp Everyone stood up respectfully when the Military Governor, having stayed about half an hour alone with the dying man, passed out, slightly acknowledging their bows and trying to escape as quickly as possible from the glances fixed on him by the doctors, clergy, and relatives of the family.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli, who had grown thinner and paler during the last few days, escorted him to the door, repeating something to him several times in low tones.
__label__twp When the Military Governor had gone, Prince Vasíli sat down all alone on a chair in the ballroom, crossing one leg high over the other, leaning his elbow on his knee and covering his face with his hand.
__label__twp After sitting so for a while he rose, and, looking about him with frightened eyes, went with unusually hurried steps down the long corridor leading to the back of the house, to the room of the eldest princess.
__label__twp Those who were in the dimly lit reception room spoke in nervous whispers, and, whenever anyone went into or came from the dying man’s room, grew silent and gazed with eyes full of curiosity or expectancy at his door, which creaked slightly when opened.
__label__twp “The limits of human life ...
__label__twp are fixed and may not be o’erpassed,” said an old priest to a lady who had taken a seat beside him and was listening naïvely to his words.
__label__twp “I wonder, is it not too late to administer unction?” asked the lady, adding the priest’s clerical title, as if she had no opinion of her own on the subject.
__label__twp “Ah, madam, it is a great sacrament,” replied the priest, passing his hand over the thin grizzled strands of hair combed back across his bald head.
__label__twp “Who was that? The Military Governor himself?” was being asked at the other side of the room.
__label__twp “How young-looking he is!”  “Yes, and he is over sixty.
__label__twp I hear the count no longer recognizes anyone.
__label__twp They wished to administer the sacrament of unction.”  “I knew someone who received that sacrament seven times.”  The second princess had just come from the sickroom with her eyes red from weeping and sat down beside Dr.
__label__twp Lorrain, who was sitting in a graceful pose under a portrait of Catherine, leaning his elbow on a table.
__label__twp “Beautiful,” said the doctor in answer to a remark about the weather.
__label__twp “The weather is beautiful, Princess; and besides, in Moscow one feels as if one were in the country.”  “Yes, indeed,” replied the princess with a sigh.
__label__twp “So he may have something to drink?”  Lorrain considered.
__label__twp “Has he taken his medicine?”  “Yes.”  The doctor glanced at his watch.
__label__twp “Take a glass of boiled water and put a pinch of cream of tartar,” and he indicated with his delicate fingers what he meant by a pinch.
__label__twp “Dere has neffer been a gase,” a German doctor was saying to an aide-de-camp, “dat one liffs after de sird stroke.”  “And what a well-preserved man he was!” remarked the aide-de-camp.
__label__twp “And who will inherit his wealth?” he added in a whisper.
__label__twp “It von’t go begging,” replied the German with a smile.
__label__twp Everyone again looked toward the door, which creaked as the second princess went in with the drink she had prepared according to Lorrain’s instructions.
__label__twp The German doctor went up to Lorrain.
__label__twp “Do you think he can last till morning?” asked the German, addressing Lorrain in French which he pronounced badly.
__label__twp Lorrain, pursing up his lips, waved a severely negative finger before his nose.
__label__twp “Tonight, not later,” said he in a low voice, and he moved away with a decorous smile of self-satisfaction at being able clearly to understand and state the patient’s condition.
__label__twp Meanwhile Prince Vasíli had opened the door into the princess’ room.
__label__twp In this room it was almost dark; only two tiny lamps were burning before the icons and there was a pleasant scent of flowers and burnt pastilles.
__label__twp The room was crowded with small pieces of furniture, whatnots, cupboards, and little tables.
__label__twp The quilt of a high, white feather bed was just visible behind a screen.
__label__twp A small dog began to bark.
__label__twp “Ah, is it you, cousin?”  She rose and smoothed her hair, which was as usual so extremely smooth that it seemed to be made of one piece with her head and covered with varnish.
__label__twp “Has anything happened?” she asked.
__label__twp “I am so terrified.”  “No, there is no change.
__label__twp I only came to have a talk about business, Catiche,” * muttered the prince, seating himself wearily on the chair she had just vacated.
__label__twp “You have made the place warm, I must say,” he remarked.
__label__twp “Well, sit down: let’s have a talk.”       *Catherine.
__label__twp “I thought perhaps something had happened,” she said with her unchanging stonily severe expression; and, sitting down opposite the prince, she prepared to listen.
__label__twp “I wished to get a nap, mon cousin, but I can’t.”  “Well, my dear?” said Prince Vasíli, taking her hand and bending it downwards as was his habit.
__label__twp It was plain that this “well?” referred to much that they both understood without naming.
__label__twp The princess, who had a straight, rigid body, abnormally long for her legs, looked directly at Prince Vasíli with no sign of emotion in her prominent gray eyes.
__label__twp Then she shook her head and glanced up at the icons with a sigh.
__label__twp This might have been taken as an expression of sorrow and devotion, or of weariness and hope of resting before long.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli understood it as an expression of weariness.
__label__twp “And I?” he said; “do you think it is easier for me? I am as worn out as a post horse, but still I must have a talk with you, Catiche, a very serious talk.”  Prince Vasíli said no more and his cheeks began to twitch nervously, now on one side, now on the other, giving his face an unpleasant expression which was never to be seen on it in a drawing room.
__label__twp His eyes too seemed strange; at one moment they looked impudently sly and at the next glanced round in alarm.
__label__twp The princess, holding her little dog on her lap with her thin bony hands, looked attentively into Prince Vasíli’s eyes evidently resolved not to be the first to break silence, if she had to wait till morning.
__label__twp “Well, you see, my dear princess and cousin, Catherine Semënovna,” continued Prince Vasíli, returning to his theme, apparently not without an inner struggle; “at such a moment as this one must think of everything.
__label__twp One must think of the future, of all of you...
__label__twp I love you all, like children of my own, as you know.”  The princess continued to look at him without moving, and with the same dull expression.
__label__twp “And then of course my family has also to be considered,” Prince Vasíli went on, testily pushing away a little table without looking at her.
__label__twp “You know, Catiche, that we—you three sisters, Mámontov, and my wife—are the count’s only direct heirs.
__label__twp I know, I know how hard it is for you to talk or think of such matters.
__label__twp It is no easier for me; but, my dear, I am getting on for sixty and must be prepared for anything.
__label__twp Do you know I have sent for Pierre? The count,” pointing to his portrait, “definitely demanded that he should be called.”  Prince Vasíli looked questioningly at the princess, but could not make out whether she was considering what he had just said or whether she was simply looking at him.
__label__twp “There is one thing I constantly pray God to grant, mon cousin,” she replied, “and it is that He would be merciful to him and would allow his noble soul peacefully to leave this...”  “Yes, yes, of course,” interrupted Prince Vasíli impatiently, rubbing his bald head and angrily pulling back toward him the little table that he had pushed away.
__label__twp “But...
__label__twp in short, the fact is...
__label__twp you know yourself that last winter the count made a will by which he left all his property, not to us his direct heirs, but to Pierre.”  “He has made wills enough!” quietly remarked the princess.
__label__twp “But he cannot leave the estate to Pierre.
__label__twp Pierre is illegitimate.”  “But, my dear,” said Prince Vasíli suddenly, clutching the little table and becoming more animated and talking more rapidly: “what if a letter has been written to the Emperor in which the count asks for Pierre’s legitimation? Do you understand that in consideration of the count’s services, his request would be granted?...”  The princess smiled as people do who think they know more about the subject under discussion than those they are talking with.
__label__twp “I can tell you more,” continued Prince Vasíli, seizing her hand, “that letter was written, though it was not sent, and the Emperor knew of it.
__label__twp The only question is, has it been destroyed or not? If not, then as soon as all is over,” and Prince Vasíli sighed to intimate what he meant by the words all is over, “and the count’s papers are opened, the will and letter will be delivered to the Emperor, and the petition will certainly be granted.
__label__twp Pierre will get everything as the legitimate son.”  “And our share?” asked the princess smiling ironically, as if anything might happen, only not that.
__label__twp “But, my poor Catiche, it is as clear as daylight! He will then be the legal heir to everything and you won’t get anything.
__label__twp You must know, my dear, whether the will and letter were written, and whether they have been destroyed or not.
__label__twp And if they have somehow been overlooked, you ought to know where they are, and must find them, because...”  “What next?” the princess interrupted, smiling sardonically and not changing the expression of her eyes.
__label__twp “I am a woman, and you think we are all stupid; but I know this: an illegitimate son cannot inherit...
__label__twp un bâtard!”* she added, as if supposing that this translation of the word would effectively prove to Prince Vasíli the invalidity of his contention.
__label__twp * A bastard.
__label__twp “Well, really, Catiche! Can’t you understand! You are so intelligent, how is it you don’t see that if the count has written a letter to the Emperor begging him to recognize Pierre as legitimate, it follows that Pierre will not be Pierre but will become Count Bezúkhov, and will then inherit everything under the will? And if the will and letter are not destroyed, then you will have nothing but the consolation of having been dutiful et tout ce qui s’ensuit!* That’s certain.”       * And all that follows therefrom.
__label__twp “I know the will was made, but I also know that it is invalid; and you, mon cousin, seem to consider me a perfect fool,” said the princess with the expression women assume when they suppose they are saying something witty and stinging.
__label__twp “My dear Princess Catherine Semënovna,” began Prince Vasíli impatiently, “I came here not to wrangle with you, but to talk about your interests as with a kinswoman, a good, kind, true relation.
__label__twp And I tell you for the tenth time that if the letter to the Emperor and the will in Pierre’s favor are among the count’s papers, then, my dear girl, you and your sisters are not heiresses! If you don’t believe me, then believe an expert.
__label__twp I have just been talking to Dmítri Onúfrich” (the family solicitor) “and he says the same.”  At this a sudden change evidently took place in the princess’ ideas; her thin lips grew white, though her eyes did not change, and her voice when she began to speak passed through such transitions as she herself evidently did not expect.
__label__twp “That would be a fine thing!” said she.
__label__twp “I never wanted anything and I don’t now.”  She pushed the little dog off her lap and smoothed her dress.
__label__twp “And this is gratitude—this is recognition for those who have sacrificed everything for his sake!” she cried.
__label__twp “It’s splendid! Fine! I don’t want anything, Prince.”  “Yes, but you are not the only one.
__label__twp There are your sisters...” replied Prince Vasíli.
__label__twp But the princess did not listen to him.
__label__twp “Yes, I knew it long ago but had forgotten.
__label__twp I knew that I could expect nothing but meanness, deceit, envy, intrigue, and ingratitude—the blackest ingratitude—in this house...”  “Do you or do you not know where that will is?” insisted Prince Vasíli, his cheeks twitching more than ever.
__label__twp “Yes, I was a fool! I still believed in people, loved them, and sacrificed myself.
__label__twp But only the base, the vile succeed! I know who has been intriguing!”  The princess wished to rise, but the prince held her by the hand.
__label__twp She had the air of one who has suddenly lost faith in the whole human race.
__label__twp She gave her companion an angry glance.
__label__twp “There is still time, my dear.
__label__twp You must remember, Catiche, that it was all done casually in a moment of anger, of illness, and was afterwards forgotten.
__label__twp Our duty, my dear, is to rectify his mistake, to ease his last moments by not letting him commit this injustice, and not to let him die feeling that he is rendering unhappy those who...”  “Who sacrificed everything for him,” chimed in the princess, who would again have risen had not the prince still held her fast, “though he never could appreciate it.
__label__twp No, mon cousin,” she added with a sigh, “I shall always remember that in this world one must expect no reward, that in this world there is neither honor nor justice.
__label__twp In this world one has to be cunning and cruel.”  “Now come, come! Be reasonable.
__label__twp I know your excellent heart.”  “No, I have a wicked heart.”  “I know your heart,” repeated the prince.
__label__twp “I value your friendship and wish you to have as good an opinion of me.
__label__twp Don’t upset yourself, and let us talk sensibly while there is still time, be it a day or be it but an hour....
__label__twp Tell me all you know about the will, and above all where it is.
__label__twp You must know.
__label__twp We will take it at once and show it to the count.
__label__twp He has, no doubt, forgotten it and will wish to destroy it.
__label__twp You understand that my sole desire is conscientiously to carry out his wishes; that is my only reason for being here.
__label__twp I came simply to help him and you.”  “Now I see it all! I know who has been intriguing—I know!” cried the princess.
__label__twp “That’s not the point, my dear.”  “It’s that protégé of yours, that sweet Princess Drubetskáya, that Anna Mikháylovna whom I would not take for a housemaid...
__label__twp the infamous, vile woman!”  “Do not let us lose any time...”  “Ah, don’t talk to me! Last winter she wheedled herself in here and told the count such vile, disgraceful things about us, especially about Sophie—I can’t repeat them—that it made the count quite ill and he would not see us for a whole fortnight.
__label__twp I know it was then he wrote this vile, infamous paper, but I thought the thing was invalid.”  “We’ve got to it at last—why did you not tell me about it sooner?”  “It’s in the inlaid portfolio that he keeps under his pillow,” said the princess, ignoring his question.
__label__twp “Now I know! Yes; if I have a sin, a great sin, it is hatred of that vile woman!” almost shrieked the princess, now quite changed.
__label__twp “And what does she come worming herself in here for? But I will give her a piece of my mind.
__label__twp The time will come!”      CHAPTER XXII  While these conversations were going on in the reception room and the princess’ room, a carriage containing Pierre (who had been sent for) and Anna Mikháylovna (who found it necessary to accompany him) was driving into the court of Count Bezúkhov’s house.
__label__twp As the wheels rolled softly over the straw beneath the windows, Anna Mikháylovna, having turned with words of comfort to her companion, realized that he was asleep in his corner and woke him up.
__label__twp Rousing himself, Pierre followed Anna Mikháylovna out of the carriage, and only then began to think of the interview with his dying father which awaited him.
__label__twp He noticed that they had not come to the front entrance but to the back door.
__label__twp While he was getting down from the carriage steps two men, who looked like tradespeople, ran hurriedly from the entrance and hid in the shadow of the wall.
__label__twp Pausing for a moment, Pierre noticed several other men of the same kind hiding in the shadow of the house on both sides.
__label__twp But neither Anna Mikháylovna nor the footman nor the coachman, who could not help seeing these people, took any notice of them.
__label__twp “It seems to be all right,” Pierre concluded, and followed Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp She hurriedly ascended the narrow dimly lit stone staircase, calling to Pierre, who was lagging behind, to follow.
__label__twp Though he did not see why it was necessary for him to go to the count at all, still less why he had to go by the back stairs, yet judging by Anna Mikháylovna’s air of assurance and haste, Pierre concluded that it was all absolutely necessary.
__label__twp Halfway up the stairs they were almost knocked over by some men who, carrying pails, came running downstairs, their boots clattering.
__label__twp These men pressed close to the wall to let Pierre and Anna Mikháylovna pass and did not evince the least surprise at seeing them there.
__label__twp “Is this the way to the princesses’ apartments?” asked Anna Mikháylovna of one of them.
__label__twp “Yes,” replied a footman in a bold loud voice, as if anything were now permissible; “the door to the left, ma’am.”  “Perhaps the count did not ask for me,” said Pierre when he reached the landing.
__label__twp “I’d better go to my own room.”  Anna Mikháylovna paused and waited for him to come up.
__label__twp “Ah, my friend!” she said, touching his arm as she had done her son’s when speaking to him that afternoon, “believe me I suffer no less than you do, but be a man!”  “But really, hadn’t I better go away?” he asked, looking kindly at her over his spectacles.
__label__twp “Ah, my dear friend! Forget the wrongs that may have been done you.
__label__twp Think that he is your father ...
__label__twp perhaps in the agony of death.” She sighed.
__label__twp “I have loved you like a son from the first.
__label__twp Trust yourself to me, Pierre.
__label__twp I shall not forget your interests.”  Pierre did not understand a word, but the conviction that all this had to be grew stronger, and he meekly followed Anna Mikháylovna who was already opening a door.
__label__twp This door led into a back anteroom.
__label__twp An old man, a servant of the princesses, sat in a corner knitting a stocking.
__label__twp Pierre had never been in this part of the house and did not even know of the existence of these rooms.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna, addressing a maid who was hurrying past with a decanter on a tray as “my dear” and “my sweet,” asked about the princess’ health and then led Pierre along a stone passage.
__label__twp The first door on the left led into the princesses’ apartments.
__label__twp The maid with the decanter in her haste had not closed the door (everything in the house was done in haste at that time), and Pierre and Anna Mikháylovna in passing instinctively glanced into the room, where Prince Vasíli and the eldest princess were sitting close together talking.
__label__twp Seeing them pass, Prince Vasíli drew back with obvious impatience, while the princess jumped up and with a gesture of desperation slammed the door with all her might.
__label__twp This action was so unlike her usual composure and the fear depicted on Prince Vasíli’s face so out of keeping with his dignity that Pierre stopped and glanced inquiringly over his spectacles at his guide.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna evinced no surprise, she only smiled faintly and sighed, as if to say that this was no more than she had expected.
__label__twp “Be a man, my friend.
__label__twp I will look after your interests,” said she in reply to his look, and went still faster along the passage.
__label__twp Pierre could not make out what it was all about, and still less what “watching over his interests” meant, but he decided that all these things had to be.
__label__twp From the passage they went into a large, dimly lit room adjoining the count’s reception room.
__label__twp It was one of those sumptuous but cold apartments known to Pierre only from the front approach, but even in this room there now stood an empty bath, and water had been spilled on the carpet.
__label__twp They were met by a deacon with a censer and by a servant who passed out on tiptoe without heeding them.
__label__twp They went into the reception room familiar to Pierre, with two Italian windows opening into the conservatory, with its large bust and full length portrait of Catherine the Great.
__label__twp The same people were still sitting here in almost the same positions as before, whispering to one another.
__label__twp All became silent and turned to look at the pale tear-worn Anna Mikháylovna as she entered, and at the big stout figure of Pierre who, hanging his head, meekly followed her.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna’s face expressed a consciousness that the decisive moment had arrived.
__label__twp With the air of a practical Petersburg lady she now, keeping Pierre close beside her, entered the room even more boldly than that afternoon.
__label__twp She felt that as she brought with her the person the dying man wished to see, her own admission was assured.
__label__twp Casting a rapid glance at all those in the room and noticing the count’s confessor there, she glided up to him with a sort of amble, not exactly bowing yet seeming to grow suddenly smaller, and respectfully received the blessing first of one and then of another priest.
__label__twp “God be thanked that you are in time,” said she to one of the priests; “all we relatives have been in such anxiety.
__label__twp This young man is the count’s son,” she added more softly.
__label__twp “What a terrible moment!”  Having said this she went up to the doctor.
__label__twp “Dear doctor,” said she, “this young man is the count’s son.
__label__twp Is there any hope?”  The doctor cast a rapid glance upwards and silently shrugged his shoulders.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna with just the same movement raised her shoulders and eyes, almost closing the latter, sighed, and moved away from the doctor to Pierre.
__label__twp To him, in a particularly respectful and tenderly sad voice, she said:  “Trust in His mercy!” and pointing out a small sofa for him to sit and wait for her, she went silently toward the door that everyone was watching and it creaked very slightly as she disappeared behind it.
__label__twp Pierre, having made up his mind to obey his monitress implicitly, moved toward the sofa she had indicated.
__label__twp As soon as Anna Mikháylovna had disappeared he noticed that the eyes of all in the room turned to him with something more than curiosity and sympathy.
__label__twp He noticed that they whispered to one another, casting significant looks at him with a kind of awe and even servility.
__label__twp A deference such as he had never before received was shown him.
__label__twp A strange lady, the one who had been talking to the priests, rose and offered him her seat; an aide-de-camp picked up and returned a glove Pierre had dropped; the doctors became respectfully silent as he passed by, and moved to make way for him.
__label__twp At first Pierre wished to take another seat so as not to trouble the lady, and also to pick up the glove himself and to pass round the doctors who were not even in his way; but all at once he felt that this would not do, and that tonight he was a person obliged to perform some sort of awful rite which everyone expected of him, and that he was therefore bound to accept their services.
__label__twp He took the glove in silence from the aide-de-camp, and sat down in the lady’s chair, placing his huge hands symmetrically on his knees in the naïve attitude of an Egyptian statue, and decided in his own mind that all was as it should be, and that in order not to lose his head and do foolish things he must not act on his own ideas tonight, but must yield himself up entirely to the will of those who were guiding him.
__label__twp Not two minutes had passed before Prince Vasíli with head erect majestically entered the room.
__label__twp He was wearing his long coat with three stars on his breast.
__label__twp He seemed to have grown thinner since the morning; his eyes seemed larger than usual when he glanced round and noticed Pierre.
__label__twp He went up to him, took his hand (a thing he never used to do), and drew it downwards as if wishing to ascertain whether it was firmly fixed on.
__label__twp “Courage, courage, my friend! He has asked to see you.
__label__twp That is well!” and he turned to go.
__label__twp But Pierre thought it necessary to ask: “How is...” and hesitated, not knowing whether it would be proper to call the dying man “the count,” yet ashamed to call him “father.”  “He had another stroke about half an hour ago.
__label__twp Courage, my friend...”  Pierre’s mind was in such a confused state that the word “stroke” suggested to him a blow from something.
__label__twp He looked at Prince Vasíli in perplexity, and only later grasped that a stroke was an attack of illness.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli said something to Lorrain in passing and went through the door on tiptoe.
__label__twp He could not walk well on tiptoe and his whole body jerked at each step.
__label__twp The eldest princess followed him, and the priests and deacons and some servants also went in at the door.
__label__twp Through that door was heard a noise of things being moved about, and at last Anna Mikháylovna, still with the same expression, pale but resolute in the discharge of duty, ran out and touching Pierre lightly on the arm said:  “The divine mercy is inexhaustible! Unction is about to be administered.
__label__twp Come.”  Pierre went in at the door, stepping on the soft carpet, and noticed that the strange lady, the aide-de-camp, and some of the servants, all followed him in, as if there were now no further need for permission to enter that room.
__label__twp CHAPTER XXIII  Pierre well knew this large room divided by columns and an arch, its walls hung round with Persian carpets.
__label__twp The part of the room behind the columns, with a high silk-curtained mahogany bedstead on one side and on the other an immense case containing icons, was brightly illuminated with red light like a Russian church during evening service.
__label__twp Under the gleaming icons stood a long invalid chair, and in that chair on snowy-white smooth pillows, evidently freshly changed, Pierre saw—covered to the waist by a bright green quilt—the familiar, majestic figure of his father, Count Bezúkhov, with that gray mane of hair above his broad forehead which reminded one of a lion, and the deep characteristically noble wrinkles of his handsome, ruddy face.
__label__twp He lay just under the icons; his large thick hands outside the quilt.
__label__twp Into the right hand, which was lying palm downwards, a wax taper had been thrust between forefinger and thumb, and an old servant, bending over from behind the chair, held it in position.
__label__twp By the chair stood the priests, their long hair falling over their magnificent glittering vestments, with lighted tapers in their hands, slowly and solemnly conducting the service.
__label__twp A little behind them stood the two younger princesses holding handkerchiefs to their eyes, and just in front of them their eldest sister, Catiche, with a vicious and determined look steadily fixed on the icons, as though declaring to all that she could not answer for herself should she glance round.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna, with a meek, sorrowful, and all-forgiving expression on her face, stood by the door near the strange lady.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli in front of the door, near the invalid chair, a wax taper in his left hand, was leaning his left arm on the carved back of a velvet chair he had turned round for the purpose, and was crossing himself with his right hand, turning his eyes upward each time he touched his forehead.
__label__twp His face wore a calm look of piety and resignation to the will of God.
__label__twp “If you do not understand these sentiments,” he seemed to be saying, “so much the worse for you!”  Behind him stood the aide-de-camp, the doctors, and the menservants; the men and women had separated as in church.
__label__twp All were silently crossing themselves, and the reading of the church service, the subdued chanting of deep bass voices, and in the intervals sighs and the shuffling of feet were the only sounds that could be heard.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna, with an air of importance that showed that she felt she quite knew what she was about, went across the room to where Pierre was standing and gave him a taper.
__label__twp He lit it and, distracted by observing those around him, began crossing himself with the hand that held the taper.
__label__twp Sophie, the rosy, laughter-loving, youngest princess with the mole, watched him.
__label__twp She smiled, hid her face in her handkerchief, and remained with it hidden for awhile; then looking up and seeing Pierre she again began to laugh.
__label__twp She evidently felt unable to look at him without laughing, but could not resist looking at him: so to be out of temptation she slipped quietly behind one of the columns.
__label__twp In the midst of the service the voices of the priests suddenly ceased, they whispered to one another, and the old servant who was holding the count’s hand got up and said something to the ladies.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna stepped forward and, stooping over the dying man, beckoned to Lorrain from behind her back.
__label__twp The French doctor held no taper; he was leaning against one of the columns in a respectful attitude implying that he, a foreigner, in spite of all differences of faith, understood the full importance of the rite now being performed and even approved of it.
__label__twp He now approached the sick man with the noiseless step of one in full vigor of life, with his delicate white fingers raised from the green quilt the hand that was free, and turning sideways felt the pulse and reflected a moment.
__label__twp The sick man was given something to drink, there was a stir around him, then the people resumed their places and the service continued.
__label__twp During this interval Pierre noticed that Prince Vasíli left the chair on which he had been leaning, and—with an air which intimated that he knew what he was about and if others did not understand him it was so much the worse for them—did not go up to the dying man, but passed by him, joined the eldest princess, and moved with her to the side of the room where stood the high bedstead with its silken hangings.
__label__twp On leaving the bed both Prince Vasíli and the princess passed out by a back door, but returned to their places one after the other before the service was concluded.
__label__twp Pierre paid no more attention to this occurrence than to the rest of what went on, having made up his mind once for all that what he saw happening around him that evening was in some way essential.
__label__twp The chanting of the service ceased, and the voice of the priest was heard respectfully congratulating the dying man on having received the sacrament.
__label__twp The dying man lay as lifeless and immovable as before.
__label__twp Around him everyone began to stir: steps were audible and whispers, among which Anna Mikháylovna’s was the most distinct.
__label__twp Pierre heard her say:  “Certainly he must be moved onto the bed; here it will be impossible...”  The sick man was so surrounded by doctors, princesses, and servants that Pierre could no longer see the reddish-yellow face with its gray mane—which, though he saw other faces as well, he had not lost sight of for a single moment during the whole service.
__label__twp He judged by the cautious movements of those who crowded round the invalid chair that they had lifted the dying man and were moving him.
__label__twp “Catch hold of my arm or you’ll drop him!” he heard one of the servants say in a frightened whisper.
__label__twp “Catch hold from underneath.
__label__twp Here!” exclaimed different voices; and the heavy breathing of the bearers and the shuffling of their feet grew more hurried, as if the weight they were carrying were too much for them.
__label__twp As the bearers, among whom was Anna Mikháylovna, passed the young man he caught a momentary glimpse between their heads and backs of the dying man’s high, stout, uncovered chest and powerful shoulders, raised by those who were holding him under the armpits, and of his gray, curly, leonine head.
__label__twp This head, with its remarkably broad brow and cheekbones, its handsome, sensual mouth, and its cold, majestic expression, was not disfigured by the approach of death.
__label__twp It was the same as Pierre remembered it three months before, when the count had sent him to Petersburg.
__label__twp But now this head was swaying helplessly with the uneven movements of the bearers, and the cold listless gaze fixed itself upon nothing.
__label__twp After a few minutes’ bustle beside the high bedstead, those who had carried the sick man dispersed.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna touched Pierre’s hand and said, “Come.” Pierre went with her to the bed on which the sick man had been laid in a stately pose in keeping with the ceremony just completed.
__label__twp He lay with his head propped high on the pillows.
__label__twp His hands were symmetrically placed on the green silk quilt, the palms downward.
__label__twp When Pierre came up the count was gazing straight at him, but with a look the significance of which could not be understood by mortal man.
__label__twp Either this look meant nothing but that as long as one has eyes they must look somewhere, or it meant too much.
__label__twp Pierre hesitated, not knowing what to do, and glanced inquiringly at his guide.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna made a hurried sign with her eyes, glancing at the sick man’s hand and moving her lips as if to send it a kiss.
__label__twp Pierre, carefully stretching his neck so as not to touch the quilt, followed her suggestion and pressed his lips to the large boned, fleshy hand.
__label__twp Neither the hand nor a single muscle of the count’s face stirred.
__label__twp Once more Pierre looked questioningly at Anna Mikháylovna to see what he was to do next.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna with her eyes indicated a chair that stood beside the bed.
__label__twp Pierre obediently sat down, his eyes asking if he were doing right.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna nodded approvingly.
__label__twp Again Pierre fell into the naïvely symmetrical pose of an Egyptian statue, evidently distressed that his stout and clumsy body took up so much room and doing his utmost to look as small as possible.
__label__twp He looked at the count, who still gazed at the spot where Pierre’s face had been before he sat down.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna indicated by her attitude her consciousness of the pathetic importance of these last moments of meeting between the father and son.
__label__twp This lasted about two minutes, which to Pierre seemed an hour.
__label__twp Suddenly the broad muscles and lines of the count’s face began to twitch.
__label__twp The twitching increased, the handsome mouth was drawn to one side (only now did Pierre realize how near death his father was), and from that distorted mouth issued an indistinct, hoarse sound.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna looked attentively at the sick man’s eyes, trying to guess what he wanted; she pointed first to Pierre, then to some drink, then named Prince Vasíli in an inquiring whisper, then pointed to the quilt.
__label__twp The eyes and face of the sick man showed impatience.
__label__twp He made an effort to look at the servant who stood constantly at the head of the bed.
__label__twp “Wants to turn on the other side,” whispered the servant, and got up to turn the count’s heavy body toward the wall.
__label__twp Pierre rose to help him.
__label__twp While the count was being turned over, one of his arms fell back helplessly and he made a fruitless effort to pull it forward.
__label__twp Whether he noticed the look of terror with which Pierre regarded that lifeless arm, or whether some other thought flitted across his dying brain, at any rate he glanced at the refractory arm, at Pierre’s terror-stricken face, and again at the arm, and on his face a feeble, piteous smile appeared, quite out of keeping with his features, that seemed to deride his own helplessness.
__label__twp At sight of this smile Pierre felt an unexpected quivering in his breast and a tickling in his nose, and tears dimmed his eyes.
__label__twp The sick man was turned on to his side with his face to the wall.
__label__twp He sighed.
__label__twp “He is dozing,” said Anna Mikháylovna, observing that one of the princesses was coming to take her turn at watching.
__label__twp “Let us go.”  Pierre went out.
__label__twp CHAPTER XXIV  There was now no one in the reception room except Prince Vasíli and the eldest princess, who were sitting under the portrait of Catherine the Great and talking eagerly.
__label__twp As soon as they saw Pierre and his companion they became silent, and Pierre thought he saw the princess hide something as she whispered:  “I can’t bear the sight of that woman.”  “Catiche has had tea served in the small drawing room,” said Prince Vasíli to Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp “Go and take something, my poor Anna Mikháylovna, or you will not hold out.”  To Pierre he said nothing, merely giving his arm a sympathetic squeeze below the shoulder.
__label__twp Pierre went with Anna Mikháylovna into the small drawing room.
__label__twp “There is nothing so refreshing after a sleepless night as a cup of this delicious Russian tea,” Lorrain was saying with an air of restrained animation as he stood sipping tea from a delicate Chinese handleless cup before a table on which tea and a cold supper were laid in the small circular room.
__label__twp Around the table all who were at Count Bezúkhov’s house that night had gathered to fortify themselves.
__label__twp Pierre well remembered this small circular drawing room with its mirrors and little tables.
__label__twp During balls given at the house Pierre, who did not know how to dance, had liked sitting in this room to watch the ladies who, as they passed through in their ball dresses with diamonds and pearls on their bare shoulders, looked at themselves in the brilliantly lighted mirrors which repeated their reflections several times.
__label__twp Now this same room was dimly lighted by two candles.
__label__twp On one small table tea things and supper dishes stood in disorder, and in the middle of the night a motley throng of people sat there, not merrymaking, but somberly whispering, and betraying by every word and movement that they none of them forgot what was happening and what was about to happen in the bedroom.
__label__twp Pierre did not eat anything though he would very much have liked to.
__label__twp He looked inquiringly at his monitress and saw that she was again going on tiptoe to the reception room where they had left Prince Vasíli and the eldest princess.
__label__twp Pierre concluded that this also was essential, and after a short interval followed her.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna was standing beside the princess, and they were both speaking in excited whispers.
__label__twp “Permit me, Princess, to know what is necessary and what is not necessary,” said the younger of the two speakers, evidently in the same state of excitement as when she had slammed the door of her room.
__label__twp “But, my dear princess,” answered Anna Mikháylovna blandly but impressively, blocking the way to the bedroom and preventing the other from passing, “won’t this be too much for poor Uncle at a moment when he needs repose? Worldly conversation at a moment when his soul is already prepared...”  Prince Vasíli was seated in an easy chair in his familiar attitude, with one leg crossed high above the other.
__label__twp His cheeks, which were so flabby that they looked heavier below, were twitching violently; but he wore the air of a man little concerned in what the two ladies were saying.
__label__twp “Come, my dear Anna Mikháylovna, let Catiche do as she pleases.
__label__twp You know how fond the count is of her.”  “I don’t even know what is in this paper,” said the younger of the two ladies, addressing Prince Vasíli and pointing to an inlaid portfolio she held in her hand.
__label__twp “All I know is that his real will is in his writing table, and this is a paper he has forgotten....”  She tried to pass Anna Mikháylovna, but the latter sprang so as to bar her path.
__label__twp “I know, my dear, kind princess,” said Anna Mikháylovna, seizing the portfolio so firmly that it was plain she would not let go easily.
__label__twp “Dear princess, I beg and implore you, have some pity on him! Je vous en conjure...”  The princess did not reply.
__label__twp Their efforts in the struggle for the portfolio were the only sounds audible, but it was evident that if the princess did speak, her words would not be flattering to Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp Though the latter held on tenaciously, her voice lost none of its honeyed firmness and softness.
__label__twp “Pierre, my dear, come here.
__label__twp I think he will not be out of place in a family consultation; is it not so, Prince?”  “Why don’t you speak, cousin?” suddenly shrieked the princess so loud that those in the drawing room heard her and were startled.
__label__twp “Why do you remain silent when heaven knows who permits herself to interfere, making a scene on the very threshold of a dying man’s room? Intriguer!” she hissed viciously, and tugged with all her might at the portfolio.
__label__twp But Anna Mikháylovna went forward a step or two to keep her hold on the portfolio, and changed her grip.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli rose.
__label__twp “Oh!” said he with reproach and surprise, “this is absurd! Come, let go I tell you.”  The princess let go.
__label__twp “And you too!”  But Anna Mikháylovna did not obey him.
__label__twp “Let go, I tell you! I will take the responsibility.
__label__twp I myself will go and ask him, I!...
__label__twp does that satisfy you?”  “But, Prince,” said Anna Mikháylovna, “after such a solemn sacrament, allow him a moment’s peace! Here, Pierre, tell them your opinion,” said she, turning to the young man who, having come quite close, was gazing with astonishment at the angry face of the princess which had lost all dignity, and at the twitching cheeks of Prince Vasíli.
__label__twp “Remember that you will answer for the consequences,” said Prince Vasíli severely.
__label__twp “You don’t know what you are doing.”  “Vile woman!” shouted the princess, darting unexpectedly at Anna Mikháylovna and snatching the portfolio from her.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli bent his head and spread out his hands.
__label__twp At this moment that terrible door, which Pierre had watched so long and which had always opened so quietly, burst noisily open and banged against the wall, and the second of the three sisters rushed out wringing her hands.
__label__twp “What are you doing!” she cried vehemently.
__label__twp “He is dying and you leave me alone with him!”  Her sister dropped the portfolio.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna, stooping, quickly caught up the object of contention and ran into the bedroom.
__label__twp The eldest princess and Prince Vasíli, recovering themselves, followed her.
__label__twp A few minutes later the eldest sister came out with a pale hard face, again biting her underlip.
__label__twp At sight of Pierre her expression showed an irrepressible hatred.
__label__twp “Yes, now you may be glad!” said she; “this is what you have been waiting for.” And bursting into tears she hid her face in her handkerchief and rushed from the room.
__label__twp Prince Vasíli came next.
__label__twp He staggered to the sofa on which Pierre was sitting and dropped onto it, covering his face with his hand.
__label__twp Pierre noticed that he was pale and that his jaw quivered and shook as if in an ague.
__label__twp “Ah, my friend!” said he, taking Pierre by the elbow; and there was in his voice a sincerity and weakness Pierre had never observed in it before.
__label__twp “How often we sin, how much we deceive, and all for what? I am near sixty, dear friend...
__label__twp I too...
__label__twp All will end in death, all! Death is awful...” and he burst into tears.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna came out last.
__label__twp She approached Pierre with slow, quiet steps.
__label__twp “Pierre!” she said.
__label__twp Pierre gave her an inquiring look.
__label__twp She kissed the young man on his forehead, wetting him with her tears.
__label__twp Then after a pause she said:  “He is no more....”  Pierre looked at her over his spectacles.
__label__twp “Come, I will go with you.
__label__twp Try to weep, nothing gives such relief as tears.”  She led him into the dark drawing room and Pierre was glad no one could see his face.
__label__twp Anna Mikháylovna left him, and when she returned he was fast asleep with his head on his arm.
__label__twp In the morning Anna Mikháylovna said to Pierre:  “Yes, my dear, this is a great loss for us all, not to speak of you.
__label__twp But God will support you: you are young, and are now, I hope, in command of an immense fortune.
__label__twp The will has not yet been opened.
__label__twp I know you well enough to be sure that this will not turn your head, but it imposes duties on you, and you must be a man.”  Pierre was silent.
__label__twp “Perhaps later on I may tell you, my dear boy, that if I had not been there, God only knows what would have happened! You know, Uncle promised me only the day before yesterday not to forget Borís.
__label__twp But he had no time.
__label__twp I hope, my dear friend, you will carry out your father’s wish?”  Pierre understood nothing of all this and coloring shyly looked in silence at Princess Anna Mikháylovna.
__label__twp After her talk with Pierre, Anna Mikháylovna returned to the Rostóvs’ and went to bed.
__label__twp On waking in the morning she told the Rostóvs and all her acquaintances the details of Count Bezúkhov’s death.
__label__twp She said the count had died as she would herself wish to die, that his end was not only touching but edifying.
__label__twp As to the last meeting between father and son, it was so touching that she could not think of it without tears, and did not know which had behaved better during those awful moments—the father who so remembered everything and everybody at last and had spoken such pathetic words to the son, or Pierre, whom it had been pitiful to see, so stricken was he with grief, though he tried hard to hide it in order not to sadden his dying father.
__label__twp “It is painful, but it does one good.
__label__twp It uplifts the soul to see such men as the old count and his worthy son,” said she.
__label__twp Of the behavior of the eldest princess and Prince Vasíli she spoke disapprovingly, but in whispers and as a great secret.
__label__twp CHAPTER XXV  At Bald Hills, Prince Nicholas Andréevich Bolkónski’s estate, the arrival of young Prince Andrew and his wife was daily expected, but this expectation did not upset the regular routine of life in the old prince’s household.
__label__twp General in Chief Prince Nicholas Andréevich (nicknamed in society, “the King of Prussia”) ever since the Emperor Paul had exiled him to his country estate had lived there continuously with his daughter, Princess Mary, and her companion, Mademoiselle Bourienne.
__label__twp Though in the new reign he was free to return to the capitals, he still continued to live in the country, remarking that anyone who wanted to see him could come the hundred miles from Moscow to Bald Hills, while he himself needed no one and nothing.
__label__twp He used to say that there are only two sources of human vice—idleness and superstition, and only two virtues—activity and intelligence.
__label__twp He himself undertook his daughter’s education, and to develop these two cardinal virtues in her gave her lessons in algebra and geometry till she was twenty, and arranged her life so that her whole time was occupied.
__label__twp He was himself always occupied: writing his memoirs, solving problems in higher mathematics, turning snuffboxes on a lathe, working in the garden, or superintending the building that was always going on at his estate.
__label__twp As regularity is a prime condition facilitating activity, regularity in his household was carried to the highest point of exactitude.
__label__twp He always came to table under precisely the same conditions, and not only at the same hour but at the same minute.
__label__twp With those about him, from his daughter to his serfs, the prince was sharp and invariably exacting, so that without being a hardhearted man he inspired such fear and respect as few hardhearted men would have aroused.
__label__twp Although he was in retirement and had now no influence in political affairs, every high official appointed to the province in which the prince’s estate lay considered it his duty to visit him and waited in the lofty antechamber just as the architect, gardener, or Princess Mary did, till the prince appeared punctually to the appointed hour.
__label__twp Everyone sitting in this antechamber experienced the same feeling of respect and even fear when the enormously high study door opened and showed the figure of a rather small old man, with powdered wig, small withered hands, and bushy gray eyebrows which, when he frowned, sometimes hid the gleam of his shrewd, youthfully glittering eyes.
__label__twp On the morning of the day that the young couple were to arrive, Princess Mary entered the antechamber as usual at the time appointed for the morning greeting, crossing herself with trepidation and repeating a silent prayer.
__label__twp Every morning she came in like that, and every morning prayed that the daily interview might pass off well.
__label__twp An old powdered manservant who was sitting in the antechamber rose quietly and said in a whisper: “Please walk in.”  Through the door came the regular hum of a lathe.
__label__twp The princess timidly opened the door which moved noiselessly and easily.
__label__twp She paused at the entrance.
