__label__twp The prince was working at the lathe and after glancing round continued his work.
__label__twp The enormous study was full of things evidently in constant use.
__label__twp The large table covered with books and plans, the tall glass-fronted bookcases with keys in the locks, the high desk for writing while standing up, on which lay an open exercise book, and the lathe with tools laid ready to hand and shavings scattered around—all indicated continuous, varied, and orderly activity.
__label__twp The motion of the small foot shod in a Tartar boot embroidered with silver, and the firm pressure of the lean sinewy hand, showed that the prince still possessed the tenacious endurance and vigor of hardy old age.
__label__twp After a few more turns of the lathe he removed his foot from the pedal, wiped his chisel, dropped it into a leather pouch attached to the lathe, and, approaching the table, summoned his daughter.
__label__twp He never gave his children a blessing, so he simply held out his bristly cheek (as yet unshaven) and, regarding her tenderly and attentively, said severely:  “Quite well? All right then, sit down.” He took the exercise book containing lessons in geometry written by himself and drew up a chair with his foot.
__label__twp “For tomorrow!” said he, quickly finding the page and making a scratch from one paragraph to another with his hard nail.
__label__twp The princess bent over the exercise book on the table.
__label__twp “Wait a bit, here’s a letter for you,” said the old man suddenly, taking a letter addressed in a woman’s hand from a bag hanging above the table, onto which he threw it.
__label__twp At the sight of the letter red patches showed themselves on the princess’ face.
__label__twp She took it quickly and bent her head over it.
__label__twp “From Héloïse?” asked the prince with a cold smile that showed his still sound, yellowish teeth.
__label__twp “Yes, it’s from Julie,” replied the princess with a timid glance and a timid smile.
__label__twp “I’ll let two more letters pass, but the third I’ll read,” said the prince sternly; “I’m afraid you write much nonsense.
__label__twp I’ll read the third!”  “Read this if you like, Father,” said the princess, blushing still more and holding out the letter.
__label__twp “The third, I said the third!” cried the prince abruptly, pushing the letter away, and leaning his elbows on the table he drew toward him the exercise book containing geometrical figures.
__label__twp “Well, madam,” he began, stooping over the book close to his daughter and placing an arm on the back of the chair on which she sat, so that she felt herself surrounded on all sides by the acrid scent of old age and tobacco, which she had known so long.
__label__twp “Now, madam, these triangles are equal; please note that the angle ABC...”  The princess looked in a scared way at her father’s eyes glittering close to her; the red patches on her face came and went, and it was plain that she understood nothing and was so frightened that her fear would prevent her understanding any of her father’s further explanations, however clear they might be.
__label__twp Whether it was the teacher’s fault or the pupil’s, this same thing happened every day: the princess’ eyes grew dim, she could not see and could not hear anything, but was only conscious of her stern father’s withered face close to her, of his breath and the smell of him, and could think only of how to get away quickly to her own room to make out the problem in peace.
__label__twp The old man was beside himself: moved the chair on which he was sitting noisily backward and forward, made efforts to control himself and not become vehement, but almost always did become vehement, scolded, and sometimes flung the exercise book away.
__label__twp The princess gave a wrong answer.
__label__twp “Well now, isn’t she a fool!” shouted the prince, pushing the book aside and turning sharply away; but rising immediately, he paced up and down, lightly touched his daughter’s hair and sat down again.
__label__twp He drew up his chair, and continued to explain.
__label__twp “This won’t do, Princess; it won’t do,” said he, when Princess Mary, having taken and closed the exercise book with the next day’s lesson, was about to leave: “Mathematics are most important, madam! I don’t want to have you like our silly ladies.
__label__twp Get used to it and you’ll like it,” and he patted her cheek.
__label__twp “It will drive all the nonsense out of your head.”  She turned to go, but he stopped her with a gesture and took an uncut book from the high desk.
__label__twp “Here is some sort of Key to the Mysteries that your Héloïse has sent you.
__label__twp Religious! I don’t interfere with anyone’s belief...
__label__twp I have looked at it.
__label__twp Take it.
__label__twp Well, now go.
__label__twp Go.”  He patted her on the shoulder and himself closed the door after her.
__label__twp Princess Mary went back to her room with the sad, scared expression that rarely left her and which made her plain, sickly face yet plainer.
__label__twp She sat down at her writing table, on which stood miniature portraits and which was littered with books and papers.
__label__twp The princess was as untidy as her father was tidy.
__label__twp She put down the geometry book and eagerly broke the seal of her letter.
__label__twp It was from her most intimate friend from childhood; that same Julie Karágina who had been at the Rostóvs’ name-day party.
__label__twp Julie wrote in French:  Dear and precious Friend, How terrible and frightful a thing is separation! Though I tell myself that half my life and half my happiness are wrapped up in you, and that in spite of the distance separating us our hearts are united by indissoluble bonds, my heart rebels against fate and in spite of the pleasures and distractions around me I cannot overcome a certain secret sorrow that has been in my heart ever since we parted.
__label__twp Why are we not together as we were last summer, in your big study, on the blue sofa, the confidential sofa? Why cannot I now, as three months ago, draw fresh moral strength from your look, so gentle, calm, and penetrating, a look I loved so well and seem to see before me as I write?  Having read thus far, Princess Mary sighed and glanced into the mirror which stood on her right.
__label__twp It reflected a weak, ungraceful figure and thin face.
__label__twp Her eyes, always sad, now looked with particular hopelessness at her reflection in the glass.
__label__twp “She flatters me,” thought the princess, turning away and continuing to read.
__label__twp But Julie did not flatter her friend, the princess’ eyes—large, deep and luminous (it seemed as if at times there radiated from them shafts of warm light)—were so beautiful that very often in spite of the plainness of her face they gave her an attraction more powerful than that of beauty.
__label__twp But the princess never saw the beautiful expression of her own eyes—the look they had when she was not thinking of herself.
__label__twp As with everyone, her face assumed a forced unnatural expression as soon as she looked in a glass.
__label__twp She went on reading:  All Moscow talks of nothing but war.
__label__twp One of my two brothers is already abroad, the other is with the Guards, who are starting on their march to the frontier.
__label__twp Our dear Emperor has left Petersburg and it is thought intends to expose his precious person to the chances of war.
__label__twp God grant that the Corsican monster who is destroying the peace of Europe may be overthrown by the angel whom it has pleased the Almighty, in His goodness, to give us as sovereign! To say nothing of my brothers, this war has deprived me of one of the associations nearest my heart.
__label__twp I mean young Nicholas Rostóv, who with his enthusiasm could not bear to remain inactive and has left the university to join the army.
__label__twp I will confess to you, dear Mary, that in spite of his extreme youth his departure for the army was a great grief to me.
__label__twp This young man, of whom I spoke to you last summer, is so noble-minded and full of that real youthfulness which one seldom finds nowadays among our old men of twenty and, particularly, he is so frank and has so much heart.
__label__twp He is so pure and poetic that my relations with him, transient as they were, have been one of the sweetest comforts to my poor heart, which has already suffered so much.
__label__twp Someday I will tell you about our parting and all that was said then.
__label__twp That is still too fresh.
__label__twp Ah, dear friend, you are happy not to know these poignant joys and sorrows.
__label__twp You are fortunate, for the latter are generally the stronger! I know very well that Count Nicholas is too young ever to be more to me than a friend, but this sweet friendship, this poetic and pure intimacy, were what my heart needed.
__label__twp But enough of this! The chief news, about which all Moscow gossips, is the death of old Count Bezúkhov, and his inheritance.
__label__twp Fancy! The three princesses have received very little, Prince Vasíli nothing, and it is Monsieur Pierre who has inherited all the property and has besides been recognized as legitimate; so that he is now Count Bezúkhov and possessor of the finest fortune in Russia.
__label__twp It is rumored that Prince Vasíli played a very despicable part in this affair and that he returned to Petersburg quite crestfallen.
__label__twp I confess I understand very little about all these matters of wills and inheritance; but I do know that since this young man, whom we all used to know as plain Monsieur Pierre, has become Count Bezúkhov and the owner of one of the largest fortunes in Russia, I am much amused to watch the change in the tone and manners of the mammas burdened by marriageable daughters, and of the young ladies themselves, toward him, though, between you and me, he always seemed to me a poor sort of fellow.
__label__twp As for the past two years people have amused themselves by finding husbands for me (most of whom I don’t even know), the matchmaking chronicles of Moscow now speak of me as the future Countess Bezúkhova.
__label__twp But you will understand that I have no desire for the post.
__label__twp À propos of marriages: do you know that a while ago that universal auntie Anna Mikháylovna told me, under the seal of strict secrecy, of a plan of marriage for you.
__label__twp It is neither more nor less than with Prince Vasíli’s son Anatole, whom they wish to reform by marrying him to someone rich and distinguée, and it is on you that his relations’ choice has fallen.
__label__twp I don’t know what you will think of it, but I consider it my duty to let you know of it.
__label__twp He is said to be very handsome and a terrible scapegrace.
__label__twp That is all I have been able to find out about him.
__label__twp But enough of gossip.
__label__twp I am at the end of my second sheet of paper, and Mamma has sent for me to go and dine at the Apráksins’.
__label__twp Read the mystical book I am sending you; it has an enormous success here.
__label__twp Though there are things in it difficult for the feeble human mind to grasp, it is an admirable book which calms and elevates the soul.
__label__twp Adieu! Give my respects to monsieur your father and my compliments to Mademoiselle Bourienne.
__label__twp I embrace you as I love you.
__label__twp JULIE  P.S.
__label__twp Let me have news of your brother and his charming little wife.
__label__twp The princess pondered awhile with a thoughtful smile and her luminous eyes lit up so that her face was entirely transformed.
__label__twp Then she suddenly rose and with her heavy tread went up to the table.
__label__twp She took a sheet of paper and her hand moved rapidly over it.
__label__twp This is the reply she wrote, also in French:  Dear and precious Friend, Your letter of the 13th has given me great delight.
__label__twp So you still love me, my romantic Julie? Separation, of which you say so much that is bad, does not seem to have had its usual effect on you.
__label__twp You complain of our separation.
__label__twp What then should I say, if I dared complain, I who am deprived of all who are dear to me? Ah, if we had not religion to console us life would be very sad.
__label__twp Why do you suppose that I should look severely on your affection for that young man? On such matters I am only severe with myself.
__label__twp I understand such feelings in others, and if never having felt them I cannot approve of them, neither do I condemn them.
__label__twp Only it seems to me that Christian love, love of one’s neighbor, love of one’s enemy, is worthier, sweeter, and better than the feelings which the beautiful eyes of a young man can inspire in a romantic and loving young girl like yourself.
__label__twp The news of Count Bezúkhov’s death reached us before your letter and my father was much affected by it.
__label__twp He says the count was the last representative but one of the great century, and that it is his own turn now, but that he will do all he can to let his turn come as late as possible.
__label__twp God preserve us from that terrible misfortune!  I cannot agree with you about Pierre, whom I knew as a child.
__label__twp He always seemed to me to have an excellent heart, and that is the quality I value most in people.
__label__twp As to his inheritance and the part played by Prince Vasíli, it is very sad for both.
__label__twp Ah, my dear friend, our divine Saviour’s words, that it is easier for a camel to go through the eye of a needle than for a rich man to enter the Kingdom of God, are terribly true.
__label__twp I pity Prince Vasíli but am still more sorry for Pierre.
__label__twp So young, and burdened with such riches—to what temptations he will be exposed! If I were asked what I desire most on earth, it would be to be poorer than the poorest beggar.
__label__twp A thousand thanks, dear friend, for the volume you have sent me and which has such success in Moscow.
__label__twp Yet since you tell me that among some good things it contains others which our weak human understanding cannot grasp, it seems to me rather useless to spend time in reading what is unintelligible and can therefore bear no fruit.
__label__twp I never could understand the fondness some people have for confusing their minds by dwelling on mystical books that merely awaken their doubts and excite their imagination, giving them a bent for exaggeration quite contrary to Christian simplicity.
__label__twp Let us rather read the Epistles and Gospels.
__label__twp Let us not seek to penetrate what mysteries they contain; for how can we, miserable sinners that we are, know the terrible and holy secrets of Providence while we remain in this flesh which forms an impenetrable veil between us and the Eternal? Let us rather confine ourselves to studying those sublime rules which our divine Saviour has left for our guidance here below.
__label__twp Let us try to conform to them and follow them, and let us be persuaded that the less we let our feeble human minds roam, the better we shall please God, who rejects all knowledge that does not come from Him; and the less we seek to fathom what He has been pleased to conceal from us, the sooner will He vouchsafe its revelation to us through His divine Spirit.
__label__twp My father has not spoken to me of a suitor, but has only told me that he has received a letter and is expecting a visit from Prince Vasíli.
__label__twp In regard to this project of marriage for me, I will tell you, dear sweet friend, that I look on marriage as a divine institution to which we must conform.
__label__twp However painful it may be to me, should the Almighty lay the duties of wife and mother upon me I shall try to perform them as faithfully as I can, without disquieting myself by examining my feelings toward him whom He may give me for husband.
__label__twp I have had a letter from my brother, who announces his speedy arrival at Bald Hills with his wife.
__label__twp This pleasure will be but a brief one, however, for he will leave us again to take part in this unhappy war into which we have been drawn, God knows how or why.
__label__twp Not only where you are—at the heart of affairs and of the world—is the talk all of war, even here amid fieldwork and the calm of nature—which townsfolk consider characteristic of the country—rumors of war are heard and painfully felt.
__label__twp My father talks of nothing but marches and countermarches, things of which I understand nothing; and the day before yesterday during my daily walk through the village I witnessed a heartrending scene....
__label__twp It was a convoy of conscripts enrolled from our people and starting to join the army.
__label__twp You should have seen the state of the mothers, wives, and children of the men who were going and should have heard the sobs.
__label__twp It seems as though mankind has forgotten the laws of its divine Saviour, Who preached love and forgiveness of injuries—and that men attribute the greatest merit to skill in killing one another.
__label__twp Adieu, dear and kind friend; may our divine Saviour and His most Holy Mother keep you in their holy and all-powerful care!  MARY  “Ah, you are sending off a letter, Princess? I have already dispatched mine.
__label__twp I have written to my poor mother,” said the smiling Mademoiselle Bourienne rapidly, in her pleasant mellow tones and with guttural r’s.
__label__twp She brought into Princess Mary’s strenuous, mournful, and gloomy world a quite different atmosphere, careless, lighthearted, and self-satisfied.
__label__twp “Princess, I must warn you,” she added, lowering her voice and evidently listening to herself with pleasure, and speaking with exaggerated grasseyement, “the prince has been scolding Michael Ivánovich.
__label__twp He is in a very bad humor, very morose.
__label__twp Be prepared.”  “Ah, dear friend,” replied Princess Mary, “I have asked you never to warn me of the humor my father is in.
__label__twp I do not allow myself to judge him and would not have others do so.”  The princess glanced at her watch and, seeing that she was five minutes late in starting her practice on the clavichord, went into the sitting room with a look of alarm.
__label__twp Between twelve and two o’clock, as the day was mapped out, the prince rested and the princess played the clavichord.
__label__twp CHAPTER XXVI  The gray-haired valet was sitting drowsily listening to the snoring of the prince, who was in his large study.
__label__twp From the far side of the house through the closed doors came the sound of difficult passages—twenty times repeated—of a sonata by Dussek.
__label__twp Just then a closed carriage and another with a hood drove up to the porch.
__label__twp Prince Andrew got out of the carriage, helped his little wife to alight, and let her pass into the house before him.
__label__twp Old Tíkhon, wearing a wig, put his head out of the door of the antechamber, reported in a whisper that the prince was sleeping, and hastily closed the door.
__label__twp Tíkhon knew that neither the son’s arrival nor any other unusual event must be allowed to disturb the appointed order of the day.
__label__twp Prince Andrew apparently knew this as well as Tíkhon; he looked at his watch as if to ascertain whether his father’s habits had changed since he was at home last, and, having assured himself that they had not, he turned to his wife.
__label__twp “He will get up in twenty minutes.
__label__twp Let us go across to Mary’s room,” he said.
__label__twp The little princess had grown stouter during this time, but her eyes and her short, downy, smiling lip lifted when she began to speak just as merrily and prettily as ever.
__label__twp “Why, this is a palace!” she said to her husband, looking around with the expression with which people compliment their host at a ball.
__label__twp “Let’s come, quick, quick!” And with a glance round, she smiled at Tíkhon, at her husband, and at the footman who accompanied them.
__label__twp “Is that Mary practicing? Let’s go quietly and take her by surprise.”  Prince Andrew followed her with a courteous but sad expression.
__label__twp “You’ve grown older, Tíkhon,” he said in passing to the old man, who kissed his hand.
__label__twp Before they reached the room from which the sounds of the clavichord came, the pretty, fair-haired Frenchwoman, Mademoiselle Bourienne, rushed out apparently beside herself with delight.
__label__twp “Ah! what joy for the princess!” exclaimed she: “At last! I must let her know.”  “No, no, please not...
__label__twp You are Mademoiselle Bourienne,” said the little princess, kissing her.
__label__twp “I know you already through my sister-in-law’s friendship for you.
__label__twp She was not expecting us?”  They went up to the door of the sitting room from which came the sound of the oft-repeated passage of the sonata.
__label__twp Prince Andrew stopped and made a grimace, as if expecting something unpleasant.
__label__twp The little princess entered the room.
__label__twp The passage broke off in the middle, a cry was heard, then Princess Mary’s heavy tread and the sound of kissing.
__label__twp When Prince Andrew went in the two princesses, who had only met once before for a short time at his wedding, were in each other’s arms warmly pressing their lips to whatever place they happened to touch.
__label__twp Mademoiselle Bourienne stood near them pressing her hand to her heart, with a beatific smile and obviously equally ready to cry or to laugh.
__label__twp Prince Andrew shrugged his shoulders and frowned, as lovers of music do when they hear a false note.
__label__twp The two women let go of one another, and then, as if afraid of being too late, seized each other’s hands, kissing them and pulling them away, and again began kissing each other on the face, and then to Prince Andrew’s surprise both began to cry and kissed again.
__label__twp Mademoiselle Bourienne also began to cry.
__label__twp Prince Andrew evidently felt ill at ease, but to the two women it seemed quite natural that they should cry, and apparently it never entered their heads that it could have been otherwise at this meeting.
__label__twp “Ah! my dear!...
__label__twp Ah! Mary!...” they suddenly exclaimed, and then laughed.
__label__twp “I dreamed last night...”—“You were not expecting us?...” “Ah! Mary, you have got thinner?...” “And you have grown stouter!...”  “I knew the princess at once,” put in Mademoiselle Bourienne.
__label__twp “And I had no idea!...” exclaimed Princess Mary.
__label__twp “Ah, Andrew, I did not see you.”  Prince Andrew and his sister, hand in hand, kissed one another, and he told her she was still the same crybaby as ever.
__label__twp Princess Mary had turned toward her brother, and through her tears the loving, warm, gentle look of her large luminous eyes, very beautiful at that moment, rested on Prince Andrew’s face.
__label__twp The little princess talked incessantly, her short, downy upper lip continually and rapidly touching her rosy nether lip when necessary and drawing up again next moment when her face broke into a smile of glittering teeth and sparkling eyes.
__label__twp She told of an accident they had had on the Spásski Hill which might have been serious for her in her condition, and immediately after that informed them that she had left all her clothes in Petersburg and that heaven knew what she would have to dress in here; and that Andrew had quite changed, and that Kitty Odýntsova had married an old man, and that there was a suitor for Mary, a real one, but that they would talk of that later.
__label__twp Princess Mary was still looking silently at her brother and her beautiful eyes were full of love and sadness.
__label__twp It was plain that she was following a train of thought independent of her sister-in-law’s words.
__label__twp In the midst of a description of the last Petersburg fete she addressed her brother:  “So you are really going to the war, Andrew?” she said sighing.
__label__twp Lise sighed too.
__label__twp “Yes, and even tomorrow,” replied her brother.
__label__twp “He is leaving me here, God knows why, when he might have had promotion...”  Princess Mary did not listen to the end, but continuing her train of thought turned to her sister-in-law with a tender glance at her figure.
__label__twp “Is it certain?” she said.
__label__twp The face of the little princess changed.
__label__twp She sighed and said: “Yes, quite certain.
__label__twp Ah! it is very dreadful...”  Her lip descended.
__label__twp She brought her face close to her sister-in-law’s and unexpectedly again began to cry.
__label__twp “She needs rest,” said Prince Andrew with a frown.
__label__twp “Don’t you, Lise? Take her to your room and I’ll go to Father.
__label__twp How is he? Just the same?”  “Yes, just the same.
__label__twp Though I don’t know what your opinion will be,” answered the princess joyfully.
__label__twp “And are the hours the same? And the walks in the avenues? And the lathe?” asked Prince Andrew with a scarcely perceptible smile which showed that, in spite of all his love and respect for his father, he was aware of his weaknesses.
__label__twp “The hours are the same, and the lathe, and also the mathematics and my geometry lessons,” said Princess Mary gleefully, as if her lessons in geometry were among the greatest delights of her life.
__label__twp When the twenty minutes had elapsed and the time had come for the old prince to get up, Tíkhon came to call the young prince to his father.
__label__twp The old man made a departure from his usual routine in honor of his son’s arrival: he gave orders to admit him to his apartments while he dressed for dinner.
__label__twp The old prince always dressed in old-fashioned style, wearing an antique coat and powdered hair; and when Prince Andrew entered his father’s dressing room (not with the contemptuous look and manner he wore in drawing rooms, but with the animated face with which he talked to Pierre), the old man was sitting on a large leather-covered chair, wrapped in a powdering mantle, entrusting his head to Tíkhon.
__label__twp “Ah! here’s the warrior! Wants to vanquish Buonaparte?” said the old man, shaking his powdered head as much as the tail, which Tíkhon was holding fast to plait, would allow.
__label__twp “You at least must tackle him properly, or else if he goes on like this he’ll soon have us, too, for his subjects! How are you?” And he held out his cheek.
__label__twp The old man was in a good temper after his nap before dinner.
__label__twp (He used to say that a nap “after dinner was silver—before dinner, golden.”) He cast happy, sidelong glances at his son from under his thick, bushy eyebrows.
__label__twp Prince Andrew went up and kissed his father on the spot indicated to him.
__label__twp He made no reply on his father’s favorite topic—making fun of the military men of the day, and more particularly of Bonaparte.
__label__twp “Yes, Father, I have come to you and brought my wife who is pregnant,” said Prince Andrew, following every movement of his father’s face with an eager and respectful look.
__label__twp “How is your health?”  “Only fools and rakes fall ill, my boy.
__label__twp You know me: I am busy from morning till night and abstemious, so of course I am well.”  “Thank God,” said his son smiling.
__label__twp “God has nothing to do with it! Well, go on,” he continued, returning to his hobby; “tell me how the Germans have taught you to fight Bonaparte by this new science you call ‘strategy.’”  Prince Andrew smiled.
__label__twp “Give me time to collect my wits, Father,” said he, with a smile that showed that his father’s foibles did not prevent his son from loving and honoring him.
__label__twp “Why, I have not yet had time to settle down!”  “Nonsense, nonsense!” cried the old man, shaking his pigtail to see whether it was firmly plaited, and grasping his by the hand.
__label__twp “The house for your wife is ready.
__label__twp Princess Mary will take her there and show her over, and they’ll talk nineteen to the dozen.
__label__twp That’s their woman’s way! I am glad to have her.
__label__twp Sit down and talk.
__label__twp About Mikhelson’s army I understand—Tolstóy ‘s too...
__label__twp a simultaneous expedition....
__label__twp But what’s the southern army to do? Prussia is neutral...
__label__twp I know that.
__label__twp What about Austria?” said he, rising from his chair and pacing up and down the room followed by Tíkhon, who ran after him, handing him different articles of clothing.
__label__twp “What of Sweden? How will they cross Pomerania?”  Prince Andrew, seeing that his father insisted, began—at first reluctantly, but gradually with more and more animation, and from habit changing unconsciously from Russian to French as he went on—to explain the plan of operation for the coming campaign.
__label__twp He explained how an army, ninety thousand strong, was to threaten Prussia so as to bring her out of her neutrality and draw her into the war; how part of that army was to join some Swedish forces at Stralsund; how two hundred and twenty thousand Austrians, with a hundred thousand Russians, were to operate in Italy and on the Rhine; how fifty thousand Russians and as many English were to land at Naples, and how a total force of five hundred thousand men was to attack the French from different sides.
__label__twp The old prince did not evince the least interest during this explanation, but as if he were not listening to it continued to dress while walking about, and three times unexpectedly interrupted.
__label__twp Once he stopped it by shouting: “The white one, the white one!”  This meant that Tíkhon was not handing him the waistcoat he wanted.
__label__twp Another time he interrupted, saying:  “And will she soon be confined?” and shaking his head reproachfully said: “That’s bad! Go on, go on.”  The third interruption came when Prince Andrew was finishing his description.
__label__twp The old man began to sing, in the cracked voice of old age: “Malbrook s’en va-t-en guerre.
__label__twp Dieu sait quand reviendra.” *       * “Marlborough is going to the wars; God knows when he’ll      return.”   His son only smiled.
__label__twp “I don’t say it’s a plan I approve of,” said the son; “I am only telling you what it is.
__label__twp Napoleon has also formed his plan by now, not worse than this one.”  “Well, you’ve told me nothing new,” and the old man repeated, meditatively and rapidly:  “Dieu sait quand reviendra.
__label__twp Go to the dining room.”      CHAPTER XXVII  At the appointed hour the prince, powdered and shaven, entered the dining room where his daughter-in-law, Princess Mary, and Mademoiselle Bourienne were already awaiting him together with his architect, who by a strange caprice of his employer’s was admitted to table though the position of that insignificant individual was such as could certainly not have caused him to expect that honor.
__label__twp The prince, who generally kept very strictly to social distinctions and rarely admitted even important government officials to his table, had unexpectedly selected Michael Ivánovich (who always went into a corner to blow his nose on his checked handkerchief) to illustrate the theory that all men are equals, and had more than once impressed on his daughter that Michael Ivánovich was “not a whit worse than you or I.” At dinner the prince usually spoke to the taciturn Michael Ivánovich more often than to anyone else.
__label__twp In the dining room, which like all the rooms in the house was exceedingly lofty, the members of the household and the footmen—one behind each chair—stood waiting for the prince to enter.
__label__twp The head butler, napkin on arm, was scanning the setting of the table, making signs to the footmen, and anxiously glancing from the clock to the door by which the prince was to enter.
__label__twp Prince Andrew was looking at a large gilt frame, new to him, containing the genealogical tree of the Princes Bolkónski, opposite which hung another such frame with a badly painted portrait (evidently by the hand of the artist belonging to the estate) of a ruling prince, in a crown—an alleged descendant of Rúrik and ancestor of the Bolkónskis.
__label__twp Prince Andrew, looking again at that genealogical tree, shook his head, laughing as a man laughs who looks at a portrait so characteristic of the original as to be amusing.
__label__twp “How thoroughly like him that is!” he said to Princess Mary, who had come up to him.
__label__twp Princess Mary looked at her brother in surprise.
__label__twp She did not understand what he was laughing at.
__label__twp Everything her father did inspired her with reverence and was beyond question.
__label__twp “Everyone has his Achilles’ heel,” continued Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “Fancy, with his powerful mind, indulging in such nonsense!”  Princess Mary could not understand the boldness of her brother’s criticism and was about to reply, when the expected footsteps were heard coming from the study.
__label__twp The prince walked in quickly and jauntily as was his wont, as if intentionally contrasting the briskness of his manners with the strict formality of his house.
__label__twp At that moment the great clock struck two and another with a shrill tone joined in from the drawing room.
__label__twp The prince stood still; his lively glittering eyes from under their thick, bushy eyebrows sternly scanned all present and rested on the little princess.
__label__twp She felt, as courtiers do when the Tsar enters, the sensation of fear and respect which the old man inspired in all around him.
__label__twp He stroked her hair and then patted her awkwardly on the back of her neck.
__label__twp “I’m glad, glad, to see you,” he said, looking attentively into her eyes, and then quickly went to his place and sat down.
__label__twp “Sit down, sit down! Sit down, Michael Ivánovich!”  He indicated a place beside him to his daughter-in-law.
__label__twp A footman moved the chair for her.
__label__twp “Ho, ho!” said the old man, casting his eyes on her rounded figure.
__label__twp “You’ve been in a hurry.
__label__twp That’s bad!”  He laughed in his usual dry, cold, unpleasant way, with his lips only and not with his eyes.
__label__twp “You must walk, walk as much as possible, as much as possible,” he said.
__label__twp The little princess did not, or did not wish to, hear his words.
__label__twp She was silent and seemed confused.
__label__twp The prince asked her about her father, and she began to smile and talk.
__label__twp He asked about mutual acquaintances, and she became still more animated and chattered away giving him greetings from various people and retelling the town gossip.
__label__twp “Countess Apráksina, poor thing, has lost her husband and she has cried her eyes out,” she said, growing more and more lively.
__label__twp As she became animated the prince looked at her more and more sternly, and suddenly, as if he had studied her sufficiently and had formed a definite idea of her, he turned away and addressed Michael Ivánovich.
__label__twp “Well, Michael Ivánovich, our Bonaparte will be having a bad time of it.
__label__twp Prince Andrew” (he always spoke thus of his son) “has been telling me what forces are being collected against him! While you and I never thought much of him.”  Michael Ivánovich did not at all know when “you and I” had said such things about Bonaparte, but understanding that he was wanted as a peg on which to hang the prince’s favorite topic, he looked inquiringly at the young prince, wondering what would follow.
__label__twp “He is a great tactician!” said the prince to his son, pointing to the architect.
__label__twp And the conversation again turned on the war, on Bonaparte, and the generals and statesmen of the day.
__label__twp The old prince seemed convinced not only that all the men of the day were mere babies who did not know the A B C of war or of politics, and that Bonaparte was an insignificant little Frenchy, successful only because there were no longer any Potëmkins or Suvórovs left to oppose him; but he was also convinced that there were no political difficulties in Europe and no real war, but only a sort of puppet show at which the men of the day were playing, pretending to do something real.
__label__twp Prince Andrew gaily bore with his father’s ridicule of the new men, and drew him on and listened to him with evident pleasure.
__label__twp “The past always seems good,” said he, “but did not Suvórov himself fall into a trap Moreau set him, and from which he did not know how to escape?”  “Who told you that? Who?” cried the prince.
__label__twp “Suvórov!” And he jerked away his plate, which Tíkhon briskly caught.
__label__twp “Suvórov!...
__label__twp Consider, Prince Andrew.
__label__twp Two...
__label__twp Frederick and Suvórov; Moreau!...
__label__twp Moreau would have been a prisoner if Suvórov had had a free hand; but he had the Hofs-kriegs-wurst-schnapps-Rath on his hands.
__label__twp It would have puzzled the devil himself! When you get there you’ll find out what those Hofs-kriegs-wurst-Raths are! Suvórov couldn’t manage them so what chance has Michael Kutúzov? No, my dear boy,” he continued, “you and your generals won’t get on against Buonaparte; you’ll have to call in the French, so that birds of a feather may fight together.
__label__twp The German, Pahlen, has been sent to New York in America, to fetch the Frenchman, Moreau,” he said, alluding to the invitation made that year to Moreau to enter the Russian service....
__label__twp “Wonderful!...
__label__twp Were the Potëmkins, Suvórovs, and Orlóvs Germans? No, lad, either you fellows have all lost your wits, or I have outlived mine.
__label__twp May God help you, but we’ll see what will happen.
__label__twp Buonaparte has become a great commander among them! Hm!...”  “I don’t at all say that all the plans are good,” said Prince Andrew, “I am only surprised at your opinion of Bonaparte.
__label__twp You may laugh as much as you like, but all the same Bonaparte is a great general!”  “Michael Ivánovich!” cried the old prince to the architect who, busy with his roast meat, hoped he had been forgotten: “Didn’t I tell you Buonaparte was a great tactician? Here, he says the same thing.”  “To be sure, your excellency,” replied the architect.
__label__twp The prince again laughed his frigid laugh.
__label__twp “Buonaparte was born with a silver spoon in his mouth.
__label__twp He has got splendid soldiers.
__label__twp Besides he began by attacking Germans.
__label__twp And only idlers have failed to beat the Germans.
__label__twp Since the world began everybody has beaten the Germans.
__label__twp They beat no one—except one another.
__label__twp He made his reputation fighting them.”  And the prince began explaining all the blunders which, according to him, Bonaparte had made in his campaigns and even in politics.
__label__twp His son made no rejoinder, but it was evident that whatever arguments were presented he was as little able as his father to change his opinion.
__label__twp He listened, refraining from a reply, and involuntarily wondered how this old man, living alone in the country for so many years, could know and discuss so minutely and acutely all the recent European military and political events.
__label__twp “You think I’m an old man and don’t understand the present state of affairs?” concluded his father.
__label__twp “But it troubles me.
__label__twp I don’t sleep at night.
__label__twp Come now, where has this great commander of yours shown his skill?” he concluded.
__label__twp “That would take too long to tell,” answered the son.
__label__twp “Well, then go off to your Buonaparte! Mademoiselle Bourienne, here’s another admirer of that powder-monkey emperor of yours,” he exclaimed in excellent French.
__label__twp “You know, Prince, I am not a Bonapartist!”  “Dieu sait quand reviendra.” hummed the prince out of tune and, with a laugh still more so, he quitted the table.
__label__twp The little princess during the whole discussion and the rest of the dinner sat silent, glancing with a frightened look now at her father-in-law and now at Princess Mary.
__label__twp When they left the table she took her sister-in-law’s arm and drew her into another room.
__label__twp “What a clever man your father is,” said she; “perhaps that is why I am afraid of him.”  “Oh, he is so kind!” answered Princess Mary.
__label__twp CHAPTER XXVIII  Prince Andrew was to leave next evening.
__label__twp The old prince, not altering his routine, retired as usual after dinner.
__label__twp The little princess was in her sister-in-law’s room.
__label__twp Prince Andrew in a traveling coat without epaulettes had been packing with his valet in the rooms assigned to him.
__label__twp After inspecting the carriage himself and seeing the trunks put in, he ordered the horses to be harnessed.
__label__twp Only those things he always kept with him remained in his room; a small box, a large canteen fitted with silver plate, two Turkish pistols and a saber—a present from his father who had brought it from the siege of Ochákov.
__label__twp All these traveling effects of Prince Andrew’s were in very good order: new, clean, and in cloth covers carefully tied with tapes.
__label__twp When starting on a journey or changing their mode of life, men capable of reflection are generally in a serious frame of mind.
__label__twp At such moments one reviews the past and plans for the future.
__label__twp Prince Andrew’s face looked very thoughtful and tender.
__label__twp With his hands behind him he paced briskly from corner to corner of the room, looking straight before him and thoughtfully shaking his head.
__label__twp Did he fear going to the war, or was he sad at leaving his wife?—perhaps both, but evidently he did not wish to be seen in that mood, for hearing footsteps in the passage he hurriedly unclasped his hands, stopped at a table as if tying the cover of the small box, and assumed his usual tranquil and impenetrable expression.
__label__twp It was the heavy tread of Princess Mary that he heard.
__label__twp “I hear you have given orders to harness,” she cried, panting (she had apparently been running), “and I did so wish to have another talk with you alone! God knows how long we may again be parted.
__label__twp You are not angry with me for coming? You have changed so, Andrúsha,” she added, as if to explain such a question.
__label__twp She smiled as she uttered his pet name, “Andrúsha.” It was obviously strange to her to think that this stern handsome man should be Andrúsha—the slender mischievous boy who had been her playfellow in childhood.
__label__twp “And where is Lise?” he asked, answering her question only by a smile.
__label__twp “She was so tired that she has fallen asleep on the sofa in my room.
__label__twp Oh, Andrew! What a treasure of a wife you have,” said she, sitting down on the sofa, facing her brother.
__label__twp “She is quite a child: such a dear, merry child.
__label__twp I have grown so fond of her.”  Prince Andrew was silent, but the princess noticed the ironical and contemptuous look that showed itself on his face.
__label__twp “One must be indulgent to little weaknesses; who is free from them, Andrew? Don’t forget that she has grown up and been educated in society, and so her position now is not a rosy one.
__label__twp We should enter into everyone’s situation.
__label__twp Tout comprendre, c’est tout pardonner.
__label__twp * Think what it must be for her, poor thing, after what she has been used to, to be parted from her husband and be left alone in the country, in her condition! It’s very hard.”       * To understand all is to forgive all.
__label__twp Prince Andrew smiled as he looked at his sister, as we smile at those we think we thoroughly understand.
__label__twp “You live in the country and don’t think the life terrible,” he replied.
__label__twp “I...
__label__twp that’s different.
__label__twp Why speak of me? I don’t want any other life, and can’t, for I know no other.
__label__twp But think, Andrew: for a young society woman to be buried in the country during the best years of her life, all alone—for Papa is always busy, and I...
__label__twp well, you know what poor resources I have for entertaining a woman used to the best society.
__label__twp There is only Mademoiselle Bourienne....”  “I don’t like your Mademoiselle Bourienne at all,” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “No? She is very nice and kind and, above all, she’s much to be pitied.
__label__twp She has no one, no one.
__label__twp To tell the truth, I don’t need her, and she’s even in my way.
__label__twp You know I always was a savage, and now am even more so.
__label__twp I like being alone....
__label__twp Father likes her very much.
__label__twp She and Michael Ivánovich are the two people to whom he is always gentle and kind, because he has been a benefactor to them both.
__label__twp As Sterne says: ‘We don’t love people so much for the good they have done us, as for the good we have done them.’ Father took her when she was homeless after losing her own father.
__label__twp She is very good-natured, and my father likes her way of reading.
__label__twp She reads to him in the evenings and reads splendidly.”  “To be quite frank, Mary, I expect Father’s character sometimes makes things trying for you, doesn’t it?” Prince Andrew asked suddenly.
__label__twp Princess Mary was first surprised and then aghast at this question.
__label__twp “For me? For me?...
__label__twp Trying for me!...” said she.
__label__twp “He always was rather harsh; and now I should think he’s getting very trying,” said Prince Andrew, apparently speaking lightly of their father in order to puzzle or test his sister.
__label__twp “You are good in every way, Andrew, but you have a kind of intellectual pride,” said the princess, following the train of her own thoughts rather than the trend of the conversation—“and that’s a great sin.
__label__twp How can one judge Father? But even if one might, what feeling except veneration could such a man as my father evoke? And I am so contented and happy with him.
__label__twp I only wish you were all as happy as I am.”  Her brother shook his head incredulously.
__label__twp “The only thing that is hard for me...
__label__twp I will tell you the truth, Andrew...
__label__twp is Father’s way of treating religious subjects.
__label__twp I don’t understand how a man of his immense intellect can fail to see what is as clear as day, and can go so far astray.
__label__twp That is the only thing that makes me unhappy.
__label__twp But even in this I can see lately a shade of improvement.
__label__twp His satire has been less bitter of late, and there was a monk he received and had a long talk with.”  “Ah! my dear, I am afraid you and your monk are wasting your powder,” said Prince Andrew banteringly yet tenderly.
__label__twp “Ah! mon ami, I only pray, and hope that God will hear me.
__label__twp Andrew...” she said timidly after a moment’s silence, “I have a great favor to ask of you.”  “What is it, dear?”  “No—promise that you will not refuse! It will give you no trouble and is nothing unworthy of you, but it will comfort me.
__label__twp Promise, Andrúsha!...” said she, putting her hand in her reticule but not yet taking out what she was holding inside it, as if what she held were the subject of her request and must not be shown before the request was granted.
__label__twp She looked timidly at her brother.
__label__twp “Even if it were a great deal of trouble...” answered Prince Andrew, as if guessing what it was about.
__label__twp “Think what you please! I know you are just like Father.
__label__twp Think as you please, but do this for my sake! Please do! Father’s father, our grandfather, wore it in all his wars.” (She still did not take out what she was holding in her reticule.) “So you promise?”  “Of course.
__label__twp What is it?”  “Andrew, I bless you with this icon and you must promise me you will never take it off.
__label__twp Do you promise?”  “If it does not weigh a hundredweight and won’t break my neck...
__label__twp To please you...” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp But immediately, noticing the pained expression his joke had brought to his sister’s face, he repented and added: “I am glad; really, dear, I am very glad.”  “Against your will He will save and have mercy on you and bring you to Himself, for in Him alone is truth and peace,” said she in a voice trembling with emotion, solemnly holding up in both hands before her brother a small, oval, antique, dark-faced icon of the Saviour in a gold setting, on a finely wrought silver chain.
__label__twp She crossed herself, kissed the icon, and handed it to Andrew.
__label__twp “Please, Andrew, for my sake!...”  Rays of gentle light shone from her large, timid eyes.
__label__twp Those eyes lit up the whole of her thin, sickly face and made it beautiful.
__label__twp Her brother would have taken the icon, but she stopped him.
__label__twp Andrew understood, crossed himself and kissed the icon.
__label__twp There was a look of tenderness, for he was touched, but also a gleam of irony on his face.
__label__twp “Thank you, my dear.” She kissed him on the forehead and sat down again on the sofa.
__label__twp They were silent for a while.
__label__twp “As I was saying to you, Andrew, be kind and generous as you always used to be.
__label__twp Don’t judge Lise harshly,” she began.
__label__twp “She is so sweet, so good-natured, and her position now is a very hard one.”  “I do not think I have complained of my wife to you, Másha, or blamed her.
__label__twp Why do you say all this to me?”  Red patches appeared on Princess Mary’s face and she was silent as if she felt guilty.
__label__twp “I have said nothing to you, but you have already been talked to.
__label__twp And I am sorry for that,” he went on.
__label__twp The patches grew deeper on her forehead, neck, and cheeks.
__label__twp She tried to say something but could not.
__label__twp Her brother had guessed right: the little princess had been crying after dinner and had spoken of her forebodings about her confinement, and how she dreaded it, and had complained of her fate, her father-in-law, and her husband.
__label__twp After crying she had fallen asleep.
__label__twp Prince Andrew felt sorry for his sister.
__label__twp “Know this, Másha: I can’t reproach, have not reproached, and never shall reproach my wife with anything, and I cannot reproach myself with anything in regard to her; and that always will be so in whatever circumstances I may be placed.
__label__twp But if you want to know the truth...
__label__twp if you want to know whether I am happy? No! Is she happy? No! But why this is so I don’t know...”  As he said this he rose, went to his sister, and, stooping, kissed her forehead.
__label__twp His fine eyes lit up with a thoughtful, kindly, and unaccustomed brightness, but he was looking not at his sister but over her head toward the darkness of the open doorway.
__label__twp “Let us go to her, I must say good-by.
__label__twp Or—go and wake and I’ll come in a moment.
__label__twp Petrúshka!” he called to his valet: “Come here, take these away.
__label__twp Put this on the seat and this to the right.”  Princess Mary rose and moved to the door, then stopped and said: “Andrew, if you had faith you would have turned to God and asked Him to give you the love you do not feel, and your prayer would have been answered.”  “Well, maybe!” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “Go, Másha; I’ll come immediately.”  On the way to his sister’s room, in the passage which connected one wing with the other, Prince Andrew met Mademoiselle Bourienne smiling sweetly.
__label__twp It was the third time that day that, with an ecstatic and artless smile, she had met him in secluded passages.
__label__twp “Oh! I thought you were in your room,” she said, for some reason blushing and dropping her eyes.
__label__twp Prince Andrew looked sternly at her and an expression of anger suddenly came over his face.
__label__twp He said nothing to her but looked at her forehead and hair, without looking at her eyes, with such contempt that the Frenchwoman blushed and went away without a word.
__label__twp When he reached his sister’s room his wife was already awake and her merry voice, hurrying one word after another, came through the open door.
__label__twp She was speaking as usual in French, and as if after long self-restraint she wished to make up for lost time.
__label__twp “No, but imagine the old Countess Zúbova, with false curls and her mouth full of false teeth, as if she were trying to cheat old age....
__label__twp Ha, ha, ha! Mary!”  This very sentence about Countess Zúbova and this same laugh Prince Andrew had already heard from his wife in the presence of others some five times.
__label__twp He entered the room softly.
__label__twp The little princess, plump and rosy, was sitting in an easy chair with her work in her hands, talking incessantly, repeating Petersburg reminiscences and even phrases.
__label__twp Prince Andrew came up, stroked her hair, and asked if she felt rested after their journey.
__label__twp She answered him and continued her chatter.
__label__twp The coach with six horses was waiting at the porch.
__label__twp It was an autumn night, so dark that the coachman could not see the carriage pole.
__label__twp Servants with lanterns were bustling about in the porch.
__label__twp The immense house was brilliant with lights shining through its lofty windows.
__label__twp The domestic serfs were crowding in the hall, waiting to bid good-by to the young prince.
__label__twp The members of the household were all gathered in the reception hall: Michael Ivánovich, Mademoiselle Bourienne, Princess Mary, and the little princess.
__label__twp Prince Andrew had been called to his father’s study as the latter wished to say good-by to him alone.
__label__twp All were waiting for them to come out.
__label__twp When Prince Andrew entered the study the old man in his old-age spectacles and white dressing gown, in which he received no one but his son, sat at the table writing.
__label__twp He glanced round.
__label__twp “Going?” And he went on writing.
__label__twp “I’ve come to say good-by.”  “Kiss me here,” and he touched his cheek: “Thanks, thanks!”  “What do you thank me for?”  “For not dilly-dallying and not hanging to a woman’s apron strings.
__label__twp The Service before everything.
__label__twp Thanks, thanks!” And he went on writing, so that his quill spluttered and squeaked.
__label__twp “If you have anything to say, say it.
__label__twp These two things can be done together,” he added.
__label__twp “About my wife...
__label__twp I am ashamed as it is to leave her on your hands....”  “Why talk nonsense? Say what you want.”  “When her confinement is due, send to Moscow for an accoucheur....
__label__twp Let him be here....”  The old prince stopped writing and, as if not understanding, fixed his stern eyes on his son.
__label__twp “I know that no one can help if nature does not do her work,” said Prince Andrew, evidently confused.
__label__twp “I know that out of a million cases only one goes wrong, but it is her fancy and mine.
__label__twp They have been telling her things.
__label__twp She has had a dream and is frightened.”  “Hm...
__label__twp Hm...” muttered the old prince to himself, finishing what he was writing.
__label__twp “I’ll do it.”  He signed with a flourish and suddenly turning to his son began to laugh.
__label__twp “It’s a bad business, eh?”  “What is bad, Father?”  “The wife!” said the old prince, briefly and significantly.
__label__twp “I don’t understand!” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “No, it can’t be helped, lad,” said the prince.
__label__twp “They’re all like that; one can’t unmarry.
__label__twp Don’t be afraid; I won’t tell anyone, but you know it yourself.”  He seized his son by the hand with small bony fingers, shook it, looked straight into his son’s face with keen eyes which seemed to see through him, and again laughed his frigid laugh.
__label__twp The son sighed, thus admitting that his father had understood him.
__label__twp The old man continued to fold and seal his letter, snatching up and throwing down the wax, the seal, and the paper, with his accustomed rapidity.
__label__twp “What’s to be done? She’s pretty! I will do everything.
__label__twp Make your mind easy,” said he in abrupt sentences while sealing his letter.
__label__twp Andrew did not speak; he was both pleased and displeased that his father understood him.
__label__twp The old man got up and gave the letter to his son.
__label__twp “Listen!” said he; “don’t worry about your wife: what can be done shall be.
__label__twp Now listen! Give this letter to Michael Ilariónovich.
__label__twp * I have written that he should make use of you in proper places and not keep you long as an adjutant: a bad position! Tell him I remember and like him.
__label__twp Write and tell me how he receives you.
__label__twp If he is all right—serve him.
__label__twp Nicholas Bolkónski’s son need not serve under anyone if he is in disfavor.
__label__twp Now come here.”       *Kutúzov.
__label__twp He spoke so rapidly that he did not finish half his words, but his son was accustomed to understand him.
__label__twp He led him to the desk, raised the lid, drew out a drawer, and took out an exercise book filled with his bold, tall, close handwriting.
__label__twp “I shall probably die before you.
__label__twp So remember, these are my memoirs; hand them to the Emperor after my death.
__label__twp Now here is a Lombard bond and a letter; it is a premium for the man who writes a history of Suvórov’s wars.
__label__twp Send it to the Academy.
__label__twp Here are some jottings for you to read when I am gone.
__label__twp You will find them useful.”  Andrew did not tell his father that he would no doubt live a long time yet.
__label__twp He felt that he must not say it.
__label__twp “I will do it all, Father,” he said.
__label__twp “Well, now, good-by!” He gave his son his hand to kiss, and embraced him.
__label__twp “Remember this, Prince Andrew, if they kill you it will hurt me, your old father...” he paused unexpectedly, and then in a querulous voice suddenly shrieked: “but if I hear that you have not behaved like a son of Nicholas Bolkónski, I shall be ashamed!”  “You need not have said that to me, Father,” said the son with a smile.
__label__twp The old man was silent.
__label__twp “I also wanted to ask you,” continued Prince Andrew, “if I’m killed and if I have a son, do not let him be taken away from you—as I said yesterday...
__label__twp let him grow up with you....
__label__twp Please.”  “Not let the wife have him?” said the old man, and laughed.
__label__twp They stood silent, facing one another.
__label__twp The old man’s sharp eyes were fixed straight on his son’s.
__label__twp Something twitched in the lower part of the old prince’s face.
__label__twp “We’ve said good-by.
__label__twp Go!” he suddenly shouted in a loud, angry voice, opening his door.
__label__twp “What is it? What?” asked both princesses when they saw for a moment at the door Prince Andrew and the figure of the old man in a white dressing gown, spectacled and wigless, shouting in an angry voice.
__label__twp Prince Andrew sighed and made no reply.
__label__twp “Well!” he said, turning to his wife.
__label__twp And this “Well!” sounded coldly ironic, as if he were saying: “Now go through your performance.”  “Andrew, already!” said the little princess, turning pale and looking with dismay at her husband.
__label__twp He embraced her.
__label__twp She screamed and fell unconscious on his shoulder.
__label__twp He cautiously released the shoulder she leaned on, looked into her face, and carefully placed her in an easy chair.
__label__twp “Adieu, Mary,” said he gently to his sister, taking her by the hand and kissing her, and then he left the room with rapid steps.
__label__twp The little princess lay in the armchair, Mademoiselle Bourienne chafing her temples.
__label__twp Princess Mary, supporting her sister-in-law, still looked with her beautiful eyes full of tears at the door through which Prince Andrew had gone and made the sign of the cross in his direction.
__label__twp From the study, like pistol shots, came the frequent sound of the old man angrily blowing his nose.
__label__twp Hardly had Prince Andrew gone when the study door opened quickly and the stern figure of the old man in the white dressing gown looked out.
__label__twp “Gone? That’s all right!” said he; and looking angrily at the unconscious little princess, he shook his head reprovingly and slammed the door.
__label__twp BOOK TWO: 1805      CHAPTER I  In October, 1805, a Russian army was occupying the villages and towns of the Archduchy of Austria, and yet other regiments freshly arriving from Russia were settling near the fortress of Braunau and burdening the inhabitants on whom they were quartered.
__label__twp Braunau was the headquarters of the commander in chief, Kutúzov.
__label__twp On October 11, 1805, one of the infantry regiments that had just reached Braunau had halted half a mile from the town, waiting to be inspected by the commander in chief.
__label__twp Despite the un-Russian appearance of the locality and surroundings—fruit gardens, stone fences, tiled roofs, and hills in the distance—and despite the fact that the inhabitants (who gazed with curiosity at the soldiers) were not Russians, the regiment had just the appearance of any Russian regiment preparing for an inspection anywhere in the heart of Russia.
__label__twp On the evening of the last day’s march an order had been received that the commander in chief would inspect the regiment on the march.
__label__twp Though the words of the order were not clear to the regimental commander, and the question arose whether the troops were to be in marching order or not, it was decided at a consultation between the battalion commanders to present the regiment in parade order, on the principle that it is always better to “bow too low than not bow low enough.” So the soldiers, after a twenty-mile march, were kept mending and cleaning all night long without closing their eyes, while the adjutants and company commanders calculated and reckoned, and by morning the regiment—instead of the straggling, disorderly crowd it had been on its last march the day before—presented a well-ordered array of two thousand men each of whom knew his place and his duty, had every button and every strap in place, and shone with cleanliness.
__label__twp And not only externally was all in order, but had it pleased the commander in chief to look under the uniforms he would have found on every man a clean shirt, and in every knapsack the appointed number of articles, “awl, soap, and all,” as the soldiers say.
__label__twp There was only one circumstance concerning which no one could be at ease.
__label__twp It was the state of the soldiers’ boots.
__label__twp More than half the men’s boots were in holes.
__label__twp But this defect was not due to any fault of the regimental commander, for in spite of repeated demands boots had not been issued by the Austrian commissariat, and the regiment had marched some seven hundred miles.
__label__twp The commander of the regiment was an elderly, choleric, stout, and thick-set general with grizzled eyebrows and whiskers, and wider from chest to back than across the shoulders.
__label__twp He had on a brand-new uniform showing the creases where it had been folded and thick gold epaulettes which seemed to stand rather than lie down on his massive shoulders.
__label__twp He had the air of a man happily performing one of the most solemn duties of his life.
__label__twp He walked about in front of the line and at every step pulled himself up, slightly arching his back.
__label__twp It was plain that the commander admired his regiment, rejoiced in it, and that his whole mind was engrossed by it, yet his strut seemed to indicate that, besides military matters, social interests and the fair sex occupied no small part of his thoughts.
__label__twp “Well, Michael Mítrich, sir?” he said, addressing one of the battalion commanders who smilingly pressed forward (it was plain that they both felt happy).
__label__twp “We had our hands full last night.
__label__twp However, I think the regiment is not a bad one, eh?”  The battalion commander perceived the jovial irony and laughed.
__label__twp “It would not be turned off the field even on the Tsarítsin Meadow.”  “What?” asked the commander.
__label__twp At that moment, on the road from the town on which signalers had been posted, two men appeared on horse back.
__label__twp They were an aide-de-camp followed by a Cossack.
__label__twp The aide-de-camp was sent to confirm the order which had not been clearly worded the day before, namely, that the commander in chief wished to see the regiment just in the state in which it had been on the march: in their greatcoats, and packs, and without any preparation whatever.
__label__twp A member of the Hofkriegsrath from Vienna had come to Kutúzov the day before with proposals and demands for him to join up with the army of the Archduke Ferdinand and Mack, and Kutúzov, not considering this junction advisable, meant, among other arguments in support of his view, to show the Austrian general the wretched state in which the troops arrived from Russia.
__label__twp With this object he intended to meet the regiment; so the worse the condition it was in, the better pleased the commander in chief would be.
__label__twp Though the aide-de-camp did not know these circumstances, he nevertheless delivered the definite order that the men should be in their greatcoats and in marching order, and that the commander in chief would otherwise be dissatisfied.
__label__twp On hearing this the regimental commander hung his head, silently shrugged his shoulders, and spread out his arms with a choleric gesture.
__label__twp “A fine mess we’ve made of it!” he remarked.
__label__twp “There now! Didn’t I tell you, Michael Mítrich, that if it was said ‘on the march’ it meant in greatcoats?” said he reproachfully to the battalion commander.
__label__twp “Oh, my God!” he added, stepping resolutely forward.
__label__twp “Company commanders!” he shouted in a voice accustomed to command.
__label__twp “Sergeants major!...
__label__twp How soon will he be here?” he asked the aide-de-camp with a respectful politeness evidently relating to the personage he was referring to.
__label__twp “In an hour’s time, I should say.”  “Shall we have time to change clothes?”  “I don’t know, General....”  The regimental commander, going up to the line himself, ordered the soldiers to change into their greatcoats.
__label__twp The company commanders ran off to their companies, the sergeants major began bustling (the greatcoats were not in very good condition), and instantly the squares that had up to then been in regular order and silent began to sway and stretch and hum with voices.
__label__twp On all sides soldiers were running to and fro, throwing up their knapsacks with a jerk of their shoulders and pulling the straps over their heads, unstrapping their overcoats and drawing the sleeves on with upraised arms.
__label__twp In half an hour all was again in order, only the squares had become gray instead of black.
__label__twp The regimental commander walked with his jerky steps to the front of the regiment and examined it from a distance.
__label__twp “Whatever is this? This!” he shouted and stood still.
__label__twp “Commander of the third company!”  “Commander of the third company wanted by the general!...
__label__twp commander to the general...
__label__twp third company to the commander.” The words passed along the lines and an adjutant ran to look for the missing officer.
__label__twp When the eager but misrepeated words had reached their destination in a cry of: “The general to the third company,” the missing officer appeared from behind his company and, though he was a middle-aged man and not in the habit of running, trotted awkwardly stumbling on his toes toward the general.
__label__twp The captain’s face showed the uneasiness of a schoolboy who is told to repeat a lesson he has not learned.
__label__twp Spots appeared on his nose, the redness of which was evidently due to intemperance, and his mouth twitched nervously.
__label__twp The general looked the captain up and down as he came up panting, slackening his pace as he approached.
__label__twp “You will soon be dressing your men in petticoats! What is this?” shouted the regimental commander, thrusting forward his jaw and pointing at a soldier in the ranks of the third company in a greatcoat of bluish cloth, which contrasted with the others.
__label__twp “What have you been after? The commander in chief is expected and you leave your place? Eh? I’ll teach you to dress the men in fancy coats for a parade....
__label__twp Eh...?”  The commander of the company, with his eyes fixed on his superior, pressed two fingers more and more rigidly to his cap, as if in this pressure lay his only hope of salvation.
__label__twp “Well, why don’t you speak? Whom have you got there dressed up as a Hungarian?” said the commander with an austere gibe.
__label__twp “Your excellency...”  “Well, your excellency, what? Your excellency! But what about your excellency?...
__label__twp nobody knows.”  “Your excellency, it’s the officer Dólokhov, who has been reduced to the ranks,” said the captain softly.
__label__twp “Well? Has he been degraded into a field marshal, or into a soldier? If a soldier, he should be dressed in regulation uniform like the others.”  “Your excellency, you gave him leave yourself, on the march.”  “Gave him leave? Leave? That’s just like you young men,” said the regimental commander cooling down a little.
__label__twp “Leave indeed....
__label__twp One says a word to you and you...
__label__twp What?” he added with renewed irritation, “I beg you to dress your men decently.”  And the commander, turning to look at the adjutant, directed his jerky steps down the line.
__label__twp He was evidently pleased at his own display of anger and walking up to the regiment wished to find a further excuse for wrath.
__label__twp Having snapped at an officer for an unpolished badge, at another because his line was not straight, he reached the third company.
__label__twp “H-o-o-w are you standing? Where’s your leg? Your leg?” shouted the commander with a tone of suffering in his voice, while there were still five men between him and Dólokhov with his bluish-gray uniform.
__label__twp Dólokhov slowly straightened his bent knee, looking straight with his clear, insolent eyes in the general’s face.
__label__twp “Why a blue coat? Off with it...
__label__twp Sergeant major! Change his coat...
__label__twp the ras...” he did not finish.
__label__twp “General, I must obey orders, but I am not bound to endure...” Dólokhov hurriedly interrupted.
__label__twp “No talking in the ranks!...
__label__twp No talking, no talking!”  “Not bound to endure insults,” Dólokhov concluded in loud, ringing tones.
__label__twp The eyes of the general and the soldier met.
__label__twp The general became silent, angrily pulling down his tight scarf.
__label__twp “I request you to have the goodness to change your coat,” he said as he turned away.
__label__twp CHAPTER II  “He’s coming!” shouted the signaler at that moment.
__label__twp The regimental commander, flushing, ran to his horse, seized the stirrup with trembling hands, threw his body across the saddle, righted himself, drew his saber, and with a happy and resolute countenance, opening his mouth awry, prepared to shout.
__label__twp The regiment fluttered like a bird preening its plumage and became motionless.
__label__twp “Att-ention!” shouted the regimental commander in a soul-shaking voice which expressed joy for himself, severity for the regiment, and welcome for the approaching chief.
__label__twp Along the broad country road, edged on both sides by trees, came a high, light blue Viennese calèche, slightly creaking on its springs and drawn by six horses at a smart trot.
__label__twp Behind the calèche galloped the suite and a convoy of Croats.
__label__twp Beside Kutúzov sat an Austrian general, in a white uniform that looked strange among the Russian black ones.
__label__twp The calèche stopped in front of the regiment.
__label__twp Kutúzov and the Austrian general were talking in low voices and Kutúzov smiled slightly as treading heavily he stepped down from the carriage just as if those two thousand men breathlessly gazing at him and the regimental commander did not exist.
__label__twp The word of command rang out, and again the regiment quivered, as with a jingling sound it presented arms.
__label__twp Then amidst a dead silence the feeble voice of the commander in chief was heard.
__label__twp The regiment roared, “Health to your ex...
__label__twp len...
__label__twp len...
__label__twp lency!” and again all became silent.
__label__twp At first Kutúzov stood still while the regiment moved; then he and the general in white, accompanied by the suite, walked between the ranks.
__label__twp From the way the regimental commander saluted the commander in chief and devoured him with his eyes, drawing himself up obsequiously, and from the way he walked through the ranks behind the generals, bending forward and hardly able to restrain his jerky movements, and from the way he darted forward at every word or gesture of the commander in chief, it was evident that he performed his duty as a subordinate with even greater zeal than his duty as a commander.
__label__twp Thanks to the strictness and assiduity of its commander the regiment, in comparison with others that had reached Braunau at the same time, was in splendid condition.
__label__twp There were only 217 sick and stragglers.
__label__twp Everything was in good order except the boots.
__label__twp Kutúzov walked through the ranks, sometimes stopping to say a few friendly words to officers he had known in the Turkish war, sometimes also to the soldiers.
__label__twp Looking at their boots he several times shook his head sadly, pointing them out to the Austrian general with an expression which seemed to say that he was not blaming anyone, but could not help noticing what a bad state of things it was.
__label__twp The regimental commander ran forward on each such occasion, fearing to miss a single word of the commander in chief’s regarding the regiment.
__label__twp Behind Kutúzov, at a distance that allowed every softly spoken word to be heard, followed some twenty men of his suite.
__label__twp These gentlemen talked among themselves and sometimes laughed.
__label__twp Nearest of all to the commander in chief walked a handsome adjutant.
__label__twp This was Prince Bolkónski.
__label__twp Beside him was his comrade Nesvítski, a tall staff officer, extremely stout, with a kindly, smiling, handsome face and moist eyes.
__label__twp Nesvítski could hardly keep from laughter provoked by a swarthy hussar officer who walked beside him.
__label__twp This hussar, with a grave face and without a smile or a change in the expression of his fixed eyes, watched the regimental commander’s back and mimicked his every movement.
__label__twp Each time the commander started and bent forward, the hussar started and bent forward in exactly the same manner.
__label__twp Nesvítski laughed and nudged the others to make them look at the wag.
__label__twp Kutúzov walked slowly and languidly past thousands of eyes which were starting from their sockets to watch their chief.
__label__twp On reaching the third company he suddenly stopped.
__label__twp His suite, not having expected this, involuntarily came closer to him.
__label__twp “Ah, Timókhin!” said he, recognizing the red-nosed captain who had been reprimanded on account of the blue greatcoat.
__label__twp One would have thought it impossible for a man to stretch himself more than Timókhin had done when he was reprimanded by the regimental commander, but now that the commander in chief addressed him he drew himself up to such an extent that it seemed he could not have sustained it had the commander in chief continued to look at him, and so Kutúzov, who evidently understood his case and wished him nothing but good, quickly turned away, a scarcely perceptible smile flitting over his scarred and puffy face.
__label__twp “Another Ismail comrade,” said he.
__label__twp “A brave officer! Are you satisfied with him?” he asked the regimental commander.
__label__twp And the latter—unconscious that he was being reflected in the hussar officer as in a looking glass—started, moved forward, and answered: “Highly satisfied, your excellency!”  “We all have our weaknesses,” said Kutúzov smiling and walking away from him.
__label__twp “He used to have a predilection for Bacchus.”  The regimental commander was afraid he might be blamed for this and did not answer.
__label__twp The hussar at that moment noticed the face of the red-nosed captain and his drawn-in stomach, and mimicked his expression and pose with such exactitude that Nesvítski could not help laughing.
__label__twp Kutúzov turned round.
__label__twp The officer evidently had complete control of his face, and while Kutúzov was turning managed to make a grimace and then assume a most serious, deferential, and innocent expression.
__label__twp The third company was the last, and Kutúzov pondered, apparently trying to recollect something.
__label__twp Prince Andrew stepped forward from among the suite and said in French:  “You told me to remind you of the officer Dólokhov, reduced to the ranks in this regiment.”  “Where is Dólokhov?” asked Kutúzov.
__label__twp Dólokhov, who had already changed into a soldier’s gray greatcoat, did not wait to be called.
__label__twp The shapely figure of the fair-haired soldier, with his clear blue eyes, stepped forward from the ranks, went up to the commander in chief, and presented arms.
__label__twp “Have you a complaint to make?” Kutúzov asked with a slight frown.
__label__twp “This is Dólokhov,” said Prince Andrew.
__label__twp “Ah!” said Kutúzov.
__label__twp “I hope this will be a lesson to you.
__label__twp Do your duty.
__label__twp The Emperor is gracious, and I shan’t forget you if you deserve well.”  The clear blue eyes looked at the commander in chief just as boldly as they had looked at the regimental commander, seeming by their expression to tear open the veil of convention that separates a commander in chief so widely from a private.
__label__twp “One thing I ask of your excellency,” Dólokhov said in his firm, ringing, deliberate voice.
__label__twp “I ask an opportunity to atone for my fault and prove my devotion to His Majesty the Emperor and to Russia!”  Kutúzov turned away.
